Three Objections to Plato's Recollection Theory
Author presents three objections: reincarnation, genetic inheritance, and moral accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three Islamic philosophical objections to Plato's theory of recollection and assess their validity.

Core Idea
Plato's recollection theory fails because it contradicts observable inheritance and Islamic accountability.

This passage presents three powerful objections to Plato's theory of recollection, grounded in empirical observation and Islamic theological principles. The author challenges the theory's implications of reincarnation and its disregard for genetic inheritance, ultimately questioning how divine justice can function if souls inhabit multiple bodies. We will dissect each objection and understand why they pose serious problems for the Platonic view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target theory
Locate where the passage states the theory being critiqued: 'Platonic theory of recollection' (نظرية الاستذكار الافلاطونية). Understand that this theory claims all knowledge is recollection of Forms from a previous existence.
The theory is explicitly named in the first sentence.
2
Extract the first objection
The first objection: if the soul transmigrates through many bodies, it should remember past lives, but no one recalls anything beyond their current life. This is an empirical argument against reincarnation.
The objection uses the lack of evidence for past-life memories.
3
Extract the second objection
The second objection: recollection theory denies the observable fact of genetic inheritance of traits across generations. The author argues that hereditary similarities exist and are undeniable, contradicting the theory's implications.
This is based on common observation, not scripture.
4
Extract the third objection
The third objection: the theory implicitly accepts reincarnation, which makes divine reward and punishment incoherent. If the same soul does good and evil in different bodies, at which life is it judged? This undermines the concept of justice.
This objection relies on Islamic theological principles of accountability.
5
Evaluate the objections' force
Consider each objection's strength: the first uses empirical evidence, the second uses observable heredity, the third uses theological coherence. All three challenge the theory from different angles.
The objections are not merely philosophical but are rooted in Islamic worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان نظرية الاستذكار الافلاطونية وضعت امامها علامات استفهام كبيرة بدت عاجزة عن الاجابة عنها وسنعرض لأهم ما أثير حولها من اسئلة : (۱) ان نظرية الاستذكار تعني ان الروح المجردة تناسخت من عوالم سابقة وتكررت في اجساد عديدة مما يجعل النفس تكتسب خبرات كل الاجساد التي دخلت فيها سابقا. وهذا غير حاصل لان الانسان وان كان في أواخر العمر فانه لا يتذكر غير تجارب حياته الحالية وحسب ۲ ) ان الاستذكار من المثل العليا يعني انقطاع علاقات الصفات الوراثية بين الآباء والابناء وبين الاشقاء فيما بينهم وان موضوع التشابه الوراثي في الطبائع امر لا يمكن انکاره بل ان التوارث في الصفات يمكن ان يمتد لمئات من الاقارب ولعشرات من السنين ۳ ) ان نظرية الاستذكار فيها اعتراف ضمني بعودة الارواح الى الاجساد ومن ثم وجود الثواب والعقاب فلو افترضنا ان النفس عند جسد عمرو ارتكبت اعمال شريرة قبيحة وبعد ان انتقلت الى جسد زید ارتكبت اعمال خيرة محمودة وهكذا لعشرات من الاجساد التي انتقلت اليها النفس فعلى أي مرحلة من المراحل يكون حساب النفس ؟
The Platonic theory of recollection faced large question marks, appearing unable to answer them. We will present the most important questions raised about it: (1) The theory of recollection means that the abstract soul transmigrated through previous worlds and repeated in many bodies, causing the soul to acquire the experiences of all the bodies it entered previously. But this does not occur, because a person, even in old age, remembers only the experiences of his present life alone. (2) Recollection from the supreme Forms implies the interruption of hereditary trait relations between fathers and sons and between siblings, yet the subject of hereditary similarity in natures is undeniable; indeed, the inheritance of traits can extend to hundreds of relatives and tens of years. (3) The theory of recollection includes an implicit acknowledgment of the return of souls to bodies and consequently the existence of reward and punishment. So if we suppose that the soul in 'Amr's body committed evil and ugly deeds, then after it transferred to Zayd's body committed good and praiseworthy deeds, and so on for dozens of bodies that the soul entered, then at which stage would the reckoning of the soul be?
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to Plato's theory of recollection. The author introduces them as major question marks that the theory cannot answer. The first objection states that the theory implies the soul transmigrates through many bodies, but in reality, a person only remembers their current life's experiences, not past ones. The second objection argues that if all knowledge is recollection from the Forms, then genetic inheritance of traits (which is observable) would be negated, but inheritance clearly occurs. The third objection points out that the theory implicitly accepts reincarnation (tanāsukh), which leads to confusion about divine justice: if the same soul performs good and evil in different bodies, at which life is it judged? These objections rely on empirical observation (lack of past-life memories) and Islamic theological principles (rejection of reincarnation, importance of heredity, and coherent accountability). The footnote clarifies that reincarnation is a pagan doctrine without rational or scriptural support.

C) It relies on sensory experience as a source of knowledge
D) It implies the soul transmigrates through many bodies

Key Takeaway
The three objections show that Plato's theory of recollection fails on empirical and theological grounds, particularly regarding memory, heredity, and divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Critique of Plato's Theory of Recollection: Four Objections
Author presents four objections to Plato's theory of recollection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the four objections raised against Plato's theory of recollection and assess the author's conclusion.

Core Idea
Plato's theory of recollection leads to absurd consequences like reincarnation and denial of heritability, failing the test.

The author presents four objections that challenge the coherence of Plato's theory of recollection. These objections target the implications for personal identity, heredity, divine justice, and epistemology. By understanding these critiques, you will learn how to assess philosophical theories by testing their consequences against observable reality and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues that if the soul had lived in previous bodies and recollected past experiences, then humans would remember those experiences, but they only remember current life. This contradicts the theory's implication.
This objection uses empirical observation: lack of past-life memories.
2
Identify the second objection
The second objection states that the theory severs hereditary links between parents and children, as the soul's experiences from previous bodies should override genetic similarities. This contradicts observable inheritance of traits.
This objection uses biological observation.
3
Identify the third objection
The third objection points out that the theory implicitly accepts reincarnation (tanāsukh), leading to an absurdity: if the same soul commits good and evil in different bodies, at which stage is it held accountable in the afterlife?
This objection uses theological reasoning about justice.
4
Identify the fourth objection
The fourth objection, attributed to Aristotle, criticizes the theory of Forms for devaluing sensory experience while relying entirely on the intelligible world, yet failing to explain the connection between the two realms.
This objection is epistemological.
5
Synthesize and evaluate
Combine the four objections to assess the author's conclusion that the theory of recollection fails. Consider whether each objection is valid and whether the conclusion is justified.
The author concludes that the theory failed 'the exam.'

الاستذكار فمتی احست بمعنی خاص انتقلت فورا الى الحقيقة المثالية التي كانت تدركها قبل اتصالها بالبدن وغفلت عنها ( ١ ان نظرية الاستذكار الافلاطونية وضعت امامها علامات استفهام كبيرة بدت عاجزة عن الاجابة عنها وسنعرض لأهم ما أثير حولها من اسئلة : (۱) ان نظرية الاستذكار تعني ان الروح المجردة تناسخت من عوالم سابقة وتكررت في اجساد عديدة مما يجعل النفس تكتسب خبرات كل الاجساد التي دخلت فيها سابقا. وهذا غير حاصل لان الانسان وان كان في أواخر العمر فانه لا يتذكر غير تجارب حياته الحالية وحسب ۲ ) ان الاستذكار من المثل العليا يعني انقطاع علاقات الصفات الوراثية بين الآباء والابناء وبين الاشقاء فيما بينهم وان موضوع التشابه الوراثي في الطبائع امر لا يمكن انکاره بل ان التوارث في الصفات يمكن ان يمتد لمئات من الاقارب ولعشرات من السنين ۳ ) ان نظرية الاستذكار فيها اعتراف ضمني بعودة الارواح الى الاجساد ومن ثم وجود الثواب والعقاب فلو افترضنا ان النفس عند جسد عمرو ارتكبت اعمال شريرة قبيحة وبعد ان انتقلت الى جسد زید ارتكبت اعمال خيرة محمودة وهكذا لعشرات من الاجساد التي انتقلت اليها النفس فعلى أي مرحلة من المراحل يكون حساب النفس ؟ ٤) يقول ارسطو ( إن نظرية المثل أضعفت الثقة بالمحسوس واعتمدت بالكلية على عالم المثل. ومن الواضح ان للوجود الحسي أهمية لا يمكن تجاوزها في المعرفة وتحديد حقائق الاشياء . زيادة على ان افلاطون لم يبين كيفية ارتباط عالم الحس بعالم المثل والخلاصة ان نظرية المثل فشلت في قاعة الامتحان
Recollection: when it senses a particular meaning, it immediately transfers to the ideal reality that it used to perceive before its connection to the body and was oblivious to it. (1) Plato's theory of recollection has been faced with big question marks, appearing unable to answer them. We will present the most important questions raised about it: (1) The theory of recollection means that the abstract soul has transmigrated from previous worlds and repeated itself in many bodies, causing the self to acquire experiences of all bodies it entered before. This does not happen, because a person, even in old age, only remembers his current life's experiences. (2) Recollection from the ideal forms means the cutting off of hereditary trait relations between fathers and sons and between siblings, and the issue of hereditary similarity in natures is undeniable; rather, inheritance of traits can extend to hundreds of relatives and tens of years. (3) The theory of recollection contains an implicit acknowledgment of the return of souls to bodies and hence the existence of reward and punishment. Suppose the soul in Amr's body committed evil ugly deeds, then after transferring to Zayd's body it performed good praiseworthy deeds, and so on for tens of bodies the soul entered; at which stage would the soul be held accountable? (4) Aristotle says: 'The theory of Forms weakened trust in the sensible and relied entirely on the world of Forms. It is obvious that sensory existence has an importance that cannot be surpassed in knowledge and determining the realities of things. Additionally, Plato did not explain how the sensible world relates to the world of Forms.' The conclusion: the theory of Forms failed the exam.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a critical evaluation of Plato's theory of recollection (anamnesis). The author begins by stating that the theory raises major questions it cannot answer. Then four objections are listed in sequence. The first objection argues that if the soul had lived in previous bodies and recollected experiences, then humans would remember experiences from past lives, but they only remember their current life. The second objection points out that the theory denies hereditary transmission of traits, contradicting observable genetic similarities between parents and children. The third objection highlights the implicit acceptance of reincarnation (tanasukh al-arwah) in the theory, leading to an absurdity regarding divine accountability: if the same soul commits good and evil in different bodies, at which stage would it be judged? The fourth objection, attributed to Aristotle, states that the theory of Forms weakens trust in sensory experience while relying entirely on the intelligible world, yet fails to explain the connection between the sensible and the ideal. The author concludes that the theory of Forms has failed the test.

Key Takeaway
The author uses reduction to absurdity and empirical contradiction to refute Plato's theory of recollection, demonstrating a method of critique applicable in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Critique of Plato's Theory of Forms: Neglect of Sense and Unexplained Link
Author criticizes Plato's theory of Forms for neglecting sensory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key flaws Aristotle attributes to Plato's theory of Forms and evaluate whether these criticisms are sound.

Core Idea
Plato's theory of Forms fails because it neglects sensory knowledge and lacks an explanatory link between the two worlds.

In this passage, we encounter a classical critique of Plato's theory of Forms attributed to Aristotle. The criticism centers on two points: the theory's neglect of the sensible world and its failure to explain the connection between the sensory and ideal realms. Understanding this critique is crucial for appreciating later Islamic philosophical engagements with Platonism, particularly in the works of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major criticisms
Read the passage carefully and list the two specific flaws mentioned: first, the theory weakens trust in the sensible world; second, it fails to explain how the sensory world connects to the world of Forms.
These are the core of Aristotle's critique as presented here.
2
Extract the implicit assumption about knowledge
The passage states that sensible existence has 'importance that cannot be bypassed in knowledge and determining the realities of things.' This reveals an assumption that sensory knowledge is essential and cannot be ignored.
This assumption underlies the first criticism.
3
Formulate the overall conclusion
The author concludes that the theory 'failed the test.' Identify what 'test' means here: the test of providing a complete account of knowledge and reality.
The conclusion is a verdict of inadequacy.
4
Assess the fairness of the criticisms
Consider whether these criticisms are justified by Plato's own writings. For instance, Plato did engage with the sensible world in his dialogues, but the passage argues he did not explain the link sufficiently.
This step moves beyond the passage to evaluate the critique's strength.
5
Connect to Islamic philosophical tradition
Reflect on how Islamic philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina addressed similar issues. For example, Ibn Sina's theory of intuition (hads) attempts to bridge the gap between sensory and intellectual knowledge.
This shows the relevance of the critique in Islamic thought.

Source Text

Additionally, Plato did not explain how the sensory world is connected to the world of Forms. The conclusion is that the theory of Forms failed the test.

Commentary
This passage presents a criticism of Plato's theory of Forms attributed to Aristotle. The author states that the theory of Forms undermined confidence in the sensible world and depended completely on the world of Forms. The passage then notes the importance of sensory existence in knowledge and determining the realities of things, implying that the theory's neglect of the sensible is a flaw. It further criticizes Plato for not explaining the connection between the sensory world and the world of Forms. The conclusion is that the theory of Forms failed the test, meaning it could not provide a satisfactory account of knowledge. This passage is a critical evaluation of a key philosophical doctrine.

Key Takeaway
The critique shows that a philosophical theory must account for sensory experience and explain the relationship between different realms of existence.

The Sensory Theory of Knowledge: Tabula Rasa and Its Proponents
Author introduces empiricism, defines experience, and lists proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core claims of the sensory theory of knowledge and list its major proponents as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
John Locke’s tabula rasa: the mind begins blank and is filled only by experience.

This passage introduces the sensory theory of knowledge, a foundational epistemological stance that has been debated by Muslim philosophers like Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali. While Locke is famous for his blank slate analogy, the passage shows the theory predates him. Understanding this helps contrast it with Islamic theories of knowledge, where reason and revelation complement sense perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main epistemological claim
Locate the sentence in the Arabic where the author states that experience is 'the sole and greatest path to knowledge.' This is the central thesis of the sensory theory.
2
Define the blank slate (tabula rasa) concept
The passage describes the mind at birth as a 'ṣaḥīfa bayḍāʾ' (blank slate). Consider what this implies about the origin of ideas: they must all come from outside, through sensation and mental operations.
This directly opposes theories of innate ideas.
3
Trace the historical lineage of the theory
The author lists proponents from Epicurus to Wittgenstein. Notice that Locke is not the originator; the theory has ancient roots. Identify the names and their eras.
4
Compare with Islamic epistemological views
Reflect on how this purely sensory account differs from Islamic views that recognize innate knowledge (fiṭra) and rational intuition. For instance, al-Ghazali argued that sense perception alone cannot yield certain knowledge of metaphysical truths.
This step bridges the Western context to your own intellectual tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥ - النظرية الحسية في المعرفة
ومن أبرز روادها الفيلسوف الانجليزي جون لوك (١٦٣٢ - ١٧٠٤م) وهي من النظريات القديمة وقد عرضها لوك بشكل جديد فقال ( ان العقل في بداية حياة كل انسان عبارة عن صحيفة بيضاء والطفل يبدأ باكتساب المعرفة منذ ان يولد من خلال التجربة فهي السبيل الاوحد والاعظم الى المعرفة ونقصد بالتجربة سلسلة محاولات اللاحق منها يصلح السابق واي تجربة جديدة قد تغير فكرة سابقة حصلنا عليها من تجربة سابقة وان افكارنا هي نتاج احساساتنا وعملياتنا العقلية ولما كان العقل نفسه يتكون عن طريق الحس فان
١ المشهور ان مؤسس هذا المذهب هو جون لوك ولكن الحقيقة انها نظرية قديمة لها جذور تاريخية تمتد الى الفلسفة اليونانية القديمة وكان الفيلسوف أبيقور ٣٤٢ - ٢٧٠ ق م من أوائل المعتقدين بها . كما ان الفيلسوف فرانسيس بيكون سبق لوك في القول بهذه النظرية وان كان له اسلوب خاص في نظريته . ويمكن ان نضع جملة كبيرة من الفلاسفة تحت راية هذه النظرية منهم . الفرنسي بيران ١٧٦٦ - ۱۸۲٤م والفرنسي تين ۱۸۲۸ - ۱۸۹۳ م والامريكي ديوي ١٨٥٩ - ١٩٥٢م والفرنسي ياشلار ١٨٨٤ - ۱۹۱۳ والايطالي نافنتورا ۱۲۱۷ - ۱۲۲۱ م والنمساوي لودفيج فتغشتين ۱۸۸۹ - ۱۹۵۱ م على اختلاف في الطرائق والاشكال ( انظر : الموسوعة الفلسفية، عبدالرحمن بدوي، مصدر سابق
5 - The sensory theory of knowledge. Among its most prominent proponents is the English philosopher John Locke (1632-1704 CE). It is an ancient theory, and Locke presented it in a new way, saying: The mind at the beginning of every human's life is a blank slate, and the child begins to acquire knowledge from birth through experience, for it is the sole and greatest path to knowledge. By experience we mean a series of attempts where the later corrects the earlier, and any new experience may change a previous idea we obtained from a previous experience. Our ideas are the product of our sensations and mental operations. Since the mind itself is formed through sense, then ... It is well-known that the founder of this doctrine is John Locke, but in reality it is an ancient theory with historical roots extending to ancient Greek philosophy, and the philosopher Epicurus (342-270 BCE) was among the earliest believers in it. Also, Francis Bacon preceded Locke in stating this theory, though he had his own unique style. We can place a large number of philosophers under the banner of this theory, among them: the Frenchman Biran (1766-1824 CE), the Frenchman Taine (1828-1893), the American Dewey (1859-1952), the Frenchman Bachelard (1884-1913), the Italian Ventura (1217-1221 CE), and the Austrian Ludwig Wittgenstein (1889-1951), despite differences in methods and forms. (See: Philosophical Encyclopedia, Abd al-Rahman Badawi, previous source.)
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage introduces the sensory theory of knowledge, highlighting John Locke as a key proponent. The author describes Locke's concept of the mind as a blank slate (tabula rasa) at birth, with knowledge acquired through experience. Experience is defined as a series of attempts where later experiences correct earlier ones. Ideas result from sensations and mental operations. The author then notes that although Locke is commonly considered the founder, the theory has ancient roots, citing Epicurus and Francis Bacon as predecessors. A list of later philosophers who adhered to this theory is provided, including Biran, Taine, Dewey, Bachelard, Ventura, and Wittgenstein, acknowledging their differences. This passage serves to present the historical background and key figures of empiricism.

Key Terms
نظرية الحسية
صحيفة بيضاء
تجربة

D) Divine revelation

Key Takeaway
The sensory theory holds that all knowledge originates from sense experience, a view with ancient precedents and later proponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Rationalist Theory of Knowledge
Author presents the rationalist/idealist theory of knowledge and its principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four key principles of rationalist epistemology and contrast them with the role of sense experience in Islamic kalam.

Core Idea
Rationalism holds that certain knowledge comes from reason alone, independent of the senses.

This passage introduces the rationalist theory of knowledge, also known as idealism. It lists four foundational principles and argues that the intellect is the source of certain, unchanging knowledge. As a starting point, we will examine these principles and later contrast them with the epistemology of Islamic theology, where both reason and revelation are essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and find the sentence that states the source of certain knowledge according to rationalism. Is it reason or senses?
2
List the four principles
The passage lists four principles of rationalist philosophy. Write them down. Notice how each denies the primacy of the material world or sensory experience.
3
Analyze the justification for axiom stability
The passage claims that axioms (badihiyyat) are fixed and unchanging. Find the reason given for this stability. What is the basis for their certainty?
The passage says they are connected to axiomatic rational laws that know no doubt or contradiction.
4
Contrast with Islamic epistemology
Consider how Islamic scholars (mutakallimun) view the role of senses and reason. For example, in proving the existence of God, they often use both empirical observations and rational arguments. What would a mutakallim say about knowledge independent of senses?
This step requires external knowledge, but we can refer to the passage's own contrast between spiritual and practical experience.

النظرية العقلية في المعرفة وتسمى أيضا بالنظرية المثالية. إقترن المذهب العقلي بالفلاسفة افلاطون وسقراط وهيجل في الغرب وابن سينا وابن مسکویه في الشرق واقترن حديثا باسم الفيلسوف الالماني عمانوئيل كنت. وترتكز الفلسفة العقلية على المباديء الآتية: ۱ - العالم المادي ليس واقعا مطلقا. ۲ - العقل او الروح جوهر العالم. ٣ - الغائية: القائلة ان لكل ظاهرة طبيعية غاية. ٤ - المعرفة مستقلة عن التجربة الحسية. وترى المثالية أن المعرفة يقينة ثابتة أو مطلقة منبعها العقل الثابت وهي مستقلة عن الخبرة الحسية لان الانسان جوهره العقل والحواس مشكوك في صحتها ودقتها والاشياء المادية لاقيمة لها وان المعرفة مجردة عن الادراكات الحسية. ان العقل يتمكن من الادراك والحكم والتمييز والتحليل والتركيب من دون الحاجة الى الحواس. فالحقيقة كل الحقيقة تكمن في افكار العقل وليس في العالم الفيزيائي المادي. ان بدهيات المعرفة ثابتة لاتتغير مع تغير الظروف الاجتماعية أو المعطيات الثقافية لان اساسها ثابت يتصل بالقوانين العقلية البدهية التي لا تعرف الشك او التناقض. ان التجربة الروحية وليس التجربة العملية هي اساس تطور المعرفة كما ان قيم الحق والخير والجمال هي قيم عليا ثابتة ازلية مطلقة معطاة للانسان وليست من صنع البشر.
The rationalist theory of knowledge, also called the idealist theory. The rationalist school is associated with the philosophers Plato, Socrates, and Hegel in the West, and Ibn Sina and Ibn Miskawayh in the East, and recently associated with the German philosopher Immanuel Kant. The rationalist philosophy is based on the following principles: 1 - The material world is not absolute reality. 2 - The intellect or spirit is the essence of the world. 3 - Teleology: that every natural phenomenon has a purpose. 4 - Knowledge is independent of sensory experience. Idealism holds that knowledge is certain, fixed, or absolute, its source is the fixed intellect, and it is independent of sensory experience, because man's essence is intellect, and the senses are doubted in their validity and accuracy, and material things have no value, and knowledge is abstracted from sensory perceptions. The intellect is capable of perception, judgment, discrimination, analysis, and synthesis without needing the senses. The truth, all truth, lies in the ideas of the intellect, not in the physical material world. The axioms of knowledge are fixed, not changing with changes in social conditions or cultural data, because their foundation is fixed, connected to the axiomatic rational laws that know no doubt or contradiction. Spiritual experience, not practical experience, is the basis of the development of knowledge, just as the values of truth, goodness, and beauty are supreme, fixed, eternal, absolute values given to man, not made by humans.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the rationalist theory of knowledge, also called idealism. It begins by naming key figures associated with this school: Plato, Socrates, Hegel in the West, and Ibn Sina and Ibn Miskawayh in the East, and later Kant. The author then lists four principles of rationalist philosophy: (1) the material world is not absolute reality, (2) intellect or spirit is the essence of the world, (3) teleology—every natural phenomenon has a purpose, and (4) knowledge is independent of sensory experience. Following the principles, the passage elaborates on the idealist view: knowledge is certain and absolute, originating from the fixed intellect, independent of sensory experience. The essence of man is intellect, while senses are unreliable. Material things lack value. The intellect can perform all cognitive functions without senses. Truth resides in intellectual ideas, not in the physical world. Axioms of knowledge are unchanging, based on self-evident rational laws. Spiritual experience, not practical experience, drives knowledge development. Abstract values (truth, goodness, beauty) are absolute and given, not human-made.

Key Takeaway
Rationalism asserts that true knowledge is derived from the intellect alone, independent of sensory experience, and that the axioms of knowledge are fixed and absolute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Intrinsic Disclosure Contradiction
First critique of idealist epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical contradiction in the idealist claim that all knowledge has intrinsic disclosure of reality.

Core Idea
If every perception discloses reality, error becomes impossible—a self-contradiction.

The author critiques idealism's core assertion that all knowledge has intrinsic disclosure of reality. By showing that this leads to the impossibility of error, the argument reveals a fatal flaw. This lesson walks through the logic of this critique and its reliance on the principle of non-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the idealist claim
Idealism holds that all perceptions have intrinsic disclosure (al-kashf al-dhati) of a domain beyond the mind, meaning they inherently reveal reality.
This is a strong epistemological claim.
2
Derive the consequence
If every perception intrinsically discloses reality, then all knowledge must be correct. A thing cannot abandon its essential property.
The author states: 'al-shay' la yatakhalla 'an wasfihi al-dhati'.
3
Recognize counter-evidence
All thinkers admit that many human perceptions and judgments are erroneous and do not disclose reality.
The author uses this as an undeniable fact.
4
Identify the contradiction
The claim of intrinsic disclosure implies no error, but error exists. This is a direct violation of the principle of non-contradiction.
The idealist cannot maintain both positions simultaneously.
5
Apply the critique
The only escape for idealism is to abandon the property of intrinsic disclosure, which undermines its entire epistemology.
The author asks rhetorically: 'hal li-hadhihi al-falsafa min mahrab?'

And the rationalist philosophy is based on the following principles: 1- The material world is not an absolute reality. 2- The intellect or soul is the essence of the world. 3- Teleology: saying that every natural phenomenon has a purpose. 4- Knowledge is independent of sensory experience. And idealism sees that knowledge is certain, fixed, or absolute, its source being the fixed intellect, and it is independent of sensory experience because the essence of man is the intellect, and the senses are doubtful in their correctness and precision, and material things have no value, and knowledge is abstracted from sensory perceptions. The intellect is capable of perception, judgment, discrimination, analysis, and synthesis without need of the senses. So the truth, all truth, lies in the ideas of the intellect and not in the physical material world. The axioms of knowledge are fixed, not changing with changing social conditions or cultural data, because their foundation is fixed, connected to self-evident rational laws that do not know doubt or contradiction. Spiritual experience, not practical experience, is the basis of the development of knowledge, and the values of truth, goodness, and beauty are supreme, fixed, eternal, absolute values given to man and not made by humans. The role of man is to bear the values on his shoulders to achieve God's purpose on earth. And the following is observed regarding this theory: 1) Professor Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr says: if human perceptions and knowledges have the property of intrinsic disclosure of a domain beyond their limits, then all sciences and knowledges must be correct because they are disclosing by their nature and essence, and a thing does not abandon its intrinsic attribute, while all thinkers of humanity admit that much of the information and judgments that people have are erroneous perceptions and do not disclose anything about reality. So how can this be understood in light of what this theory claims that knowledge enjoys intrinsic disclosure? And does this philosophy have any escape but to abandon granting this attribute to knowledge?! 2) Human thinking possesses two types of knowledge: first: knowledge guaranteed to be correct, which is the general basis of thinking that cannot be doubted at all, like the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of existence and non-existence. Second: secondary knowledge derived from the previous knowledge, in which error occurs, as in the laws of physics and chemistry. 2) The objective reality of other persons cannot be denied. So if truth is confined to the perception and consciousness itself, considering that we do not contact anything beyond the bounds of the mind and its conscious contents, then this perception means an individual perception that does not connect with the perception and consciousness of others. And this imposes on the individual isolation from everything.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of idealist epistemology. The author first outlines the principles of rationalist philosophy, which underpin idealism: (1) the material world is not absolute, (2) intellect or soul is the essence of the world, (3) teleology, and (4) knowledge independent of sensory experience. Then the author summarizes the idealist position: knowledge is certain and absolute, originating from the intellect, independent of senses; the intellect can operate without senses; truth lies in mental ideas; axioms are fixed; spiritual experience is the basis of knowledge; values are absolute and given. After presenting this view, the author critiques it using two arguments from Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr. The first argument: if all perceptions had intrinsic disclosure of reality, then all knowledge would be correct, but we know many perceptions are erroneous. This contradiction shows idealism is false. The second argument distinguishes two types of knowledge: primary axioms (like non-contradiction) which are certain, and secondary derived knowledge which may contain error. Idealism fails to account for error in secondary knowledge. Finally, the author argues that denying objective reality of other minds leads to solipsism and isolation, which is untenable. The critique is grounded in Islamic philosophical discourse, using examples like the principle of non-contradiction, which is fundamental in logic.

مبدأ عدم التناقض

Key Takeaway
The idealist claim of intrinsic disclosure is self-defeating because it cannot account for the existence of error, violating the principle of non-contradiction.

The Solipsism Objection
Second critique: solipsism objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how idealist epistemology leads to solipsism and why this is untenable in Islamic thought.

Core Idea
If truth is only in individual perception, other minds become inaccessible—leading to solipsism.

The author's second critique targets the idealist denial of objective reality beyond the mind. By following the logical consequences, we arrive at a state where each person is isolated in their own mental world. This lesson examines that reductio and its conflict with the Islamic view of shared knowledge and community.

Guided Path
1
Identify the idealist premise
Idealism claims that truth is confined to perception and consciousness; we have no access to anything beyond the mind.
The author states: 'al-haqiqa muqtasira 'ala nafs al-idrak'.
2
Derive the consequence for knowledge of others
If truth is only in one's own perception, then each person's perception is isolated. There is no way to connect with the perceptions of others.
This is because perception does not refer to a shared external world.
3
Recognize the result: solipsism
One cannot know whether other minds exist or whether they have any reality. The individual becomes cut off from everything else.
The author says: 'hadha yafridu 'ala al-fard 'uzla 'an kull shay''.
4
Evaluate against Islamic principles
Islam affirms objective reality and communal knowledge through revelation and senses. Solipsism contradicts the possibility of shared truth and social obligations.
For example, the concept of ummah relies on shared experience and communication.
5
Formulate the critique
The idealist position leads to an absurd conclusion (solipsism), so it must be false. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The argument does not require proving other minds; it shows the premise is unacceptable.

The Self-Revelation Argument Against Idealism
Author presents his first argument against idealism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the modus tollens argument that the idealist notion of self-revelation (al-kashf al-dhati) leads to the absurd consequence that all knowledge is correct, thereby refuting idealism.

Core Idea
If knowledge self-reveals reality, error is impossible — but error exists, so self-revelation is false.

This lesson focuses on the first argument against idealism presented by Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr. The idealist theory claims that knowledge inherently reveals reality beyond itself. The author demonstrates that this claim entails the false conclusion that all human knowledge is correct, which contradicts our common experience of error. We will dissect the logical structure of this refutation as a modus tollens argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the idealist claim
The idealist claims that human perceptions and knowledges have the property of self-revelation (al-kashf al-dhati), meaning they inherently reveal a reality beyond themselves.
This is the premise under attack.
2
Extract the necessary consequence
If all knowledge is self-revelatory, then all knowledge must be correct and correspond to reality. A thing does not abandon its essential description.
The antecedent implies the consequent.
3
Contrast with observed reality
We observe that many human judgments are erroneous (mukhti'a) and do not reveal reality. This contradicts the necessary consequence.
The consequent is false.
4
Apply modus tollens
The argument is a classic modus tollens: If P then Q; Not Q; Therefore not P. Here, P is 'knowledge has self-revelation', Q is 'all knowledge is correct'. Since not Q, we conclude not P.
The idealist must abandon the claim of self-revelation.
5
Conclude the refutation
The idealist has no escape: either abandon self-revelation or admit all knowledge is correct. Both options undermine idealism.
This is a powerful internal critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلاحظ على هذه النظرية الآتي : ١ ) يقول الاستاذ محمد باقر الصدر (ان) الادراكات والمعارف البشرية اذا كانت لها خاصية الكشف الذاتي عن مجال وراء حدودها وجب ان تكون جميع العلوم والمعارف صحيحة لانها كاشفة بحكم طبيعتها وذاتها والشيء لا يتخلى عن وصفه الذاتي مع ان جميع مفكري البشرية يعترفون بأن كثيرا من المعلومات والاحكام التي لدى الناس هي ادراكات مخطئة ولا تكشف شيئا عن الواقع فكيف يفهم في ضوء ما تزعمه هذه النظرية من ان العلم يتمتع بالكشف الذاتي ؟ وهل لهذه الفلسفة من مهرب الا التنازل عن منح العلم هذه الصفة ؟! ) ۲ ( ان التفكير البشري يملك نوعين من المعارف الاول : معارف مضمونة الصحة هي القاعدة العامة للتفكير لا يمكن الشك فيها مطلقا كمبدأ عدم التناقض . ومبدأ الوجود والعدم الثاني : معارف ثانوية تستنتج من المعارف السابقة وهي التي يقع فيها الخطأ . كما في قوانين الفيزياء والكيمياء ( ٣ وهذا نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة يعني اننا لانسلم بوجود الناس الآخرين لانهم ليسوا الا تصورات ذهنية وفكرية . اذن فمع من نتكلم ؟ ولمن نحاور ونكتب ؟! ۳ ) الواقع الموضوعي والواقع المثالي ان) من الضروري ان نميز بين مسألتين احداهما مسألة وجود واقع موضوعي للادراكات والاحساسات والاخرى مطابقة هذا الواقع لما يبدو لنا في ادراكنا وحواسنا . وإذا ميزنا بينهما استطعنا أن نعرف ان احساساتنا هي عبارة عن انفعالات نفسية بالاشياء الخارجية فلابد من شيء خارجي حينما نحس وننفعل ولابد لنا من ان نتساءل هل ان) وجود الاشياء في المكان خارج العقل هو وجود مشكوك فيه أو زائف) ان من يشك في وجود العالم المستقل عنا هو مجنون بلا شك ) ٤ ٤ ) رب سؤال آخر يطرح امام المثالية وهو: هل تكون افكار الشخص الذي فقد حاستي البصر والسمع مثلا متكاملة متساوية متوازية مع افكار الشخص السوي الذي لم يفقد هاتين الحاستين ؟ ان الاجابة على هذا السؤال بأي وجه جاءت تنسف التفكير المثالي من اساسه والخلاصة : ان هذه النظرية لا يقرها العقل والمنطق السليمان وهي تلغي مسألة المعرفة الانسانية ودراسة قيمتها من ناحية موضوعية إلغاء تام لانها لا تعترف بموضوعية الفكر والادراك ووجود شيء خارج حدودهما.
And it is observed regarding this theory the following: 1) Professor Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr says that if human perceptions and knowledges have the property of self-revelation of a domain beyond their limits, then all sciences and knowledges must be correct because they are revealing by their nature and essence, and a thing does not abandon its essential description, while all thinkers of humanity acknowledge that many of the informations and judgments that people have are erroneous perceptions and do not reveal anything about reality. So how can it be understood in light of what this theory claims, that knowledge enjoys self-revelation? And does this philosophy have any escape except to abandon granting this attribute to knowledge?! 2) Human thinking possesses two types of knowledges: the first: knowledges guaranteed correct, which are the general foundation of thinking, absolutely indubitable, such as the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of existence and non-existence. The second: secondary knowledges deduced from the previous knowledges, which are where error occurs, as in the laws of physics and chemistry. This is the theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. It means that we do not acknowledge the existence of other people because they are nothing but mental and intellectual representations. So with whom do we speak? And for whom do we debate and write? 3) Objective reality and ideal reality: It is necessary to distinguish between two issues: one is the issue of the existence of an objective reality for perceptions and sensations, and the other is the correspondence of this reality to what appears to us in our perception and senses. If we distinguish between them, we can know that our sensations are merely psychological reactions to external things. So there must be something external when we sense and react, and we must question whether the existence of things in space outside the mind is a doubtful or false existence. Whoever doubts the existence of the world independent of us is undoubtedly insane. 4) Another question may be posed before idealism: Are the ideas of a person who has lost, for example, the senses of sight and hearing, complete, equal, and parallel to the ideas of a healthy person who has not lost these two senses? The answer to this question, whichever way it comes, demolishes idealist thinking from its foundation. The conclusion: This theory is not approved by sound intellect and logic, and it completely abolishes the issue of human knowledge and the study of its value from an objective perspective because it does not recognize the objectivity of thought and perception and the existence of anything outside their limits.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of idealist epistemology, which claims that knowledge is merely subjective and does not correspond to an external reality. The author presents four distinct arguments against this theory. The first argument, quoted from Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr, attacks the idealist notion that knowledge has 'self-revelation' (al-kashf al-dhati). If knowledge inherently reveals reality, then all knowledge would be correct. Yet we know that many human judgments are erroneous. Therefore, the idealist claim is self-contradictory; it must either abandon the attribute of self-revelation or concede that all knowledge is correct, which is false. The second argument points out that human thinking includes both certain, foundational principles (like the law of non-contradiction) and secondary, derived knowledge where error occurs. Idealism fails to account for this distinction. The third argument draws from the theory of knowledge between Quran and philosophy: idealism leads to solipsism, denying the existence of other minds. If everything is mental representations, then we have no basis for communication with others. The fourth argument concerns objective versus ideal reality. The author distinguishes between the existence of an objective reality for perceptions and the correspondence of that reality to our senses. Our sensations are reactions to external things, so there must be an external world. Doubting this is insanity. Finally, the fifth argument (numbered 4) asks whether a person deprived of senses would have the same ideas as a normal person. The answer, whichever way, undermines idealism because it shows that sensory input shapes knowledge, implying an external source. The conclusion is that idealism is rejected by sound intellect and logic, as it abolishes objective knowledge.

Key Terms
الكشف الذاتي
المثالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument against idealism, what logical form does the refutation take?
A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens
C) Disjunctive syllogism
D) Affirming the consequent

Key Takeaway
The existence of error disproves the idealist claim that knowledge inherently reveals reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar modus tollens argument against someone who claims that all interpretations of the Quran are equally valid. Assume the claim 'every interpretation is correct' leads to contradictions like mutually exclusive rulings being both correct.
?
Identify the antecedent and consequent in that argument and check whether the modus tollens is valid.

The Two Types of Knowledge Argument
Author presents second argument against idealism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between certain foundational principles (like the law of non-contradiction) and fallible secondary knowledge, and explain how idealism fails to account for this distinction.

Core Idea
Idealism cannot explain why some knowledge is absolutely certain and other knowledge is fallible.

The second argument against idealism highlights a fundamental distinction in human knowledge: there are primary, indubitable principles (like the law of non-contradiction and the principle of existence/non-existence) and secondary, derived knowledge that is fallible (e.g., laws of physics and chemistry). Idealism, by denying objective reality and making knowledge subjective, cannot explain why some knowledge is immune from error while other knowledge is not. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of knowledge
The author states that human thinking possesses two types: first, knowledges guaranteed correct (ma'arif madmunat al-sihha), which are the foundation of reasoning and absolutely certain; second, secondary knowledges (ma'arif thanawiyya) derived from the first, where error occurs.
Examples given: principle of non-contradiction, principle of existence/non-existence vs. laws of physics/chemistry.
2
Understand the nature of the first type
These are self-evident, axiomatic truths that cannot be doubted. They are the bedrock of all reasoning and are universally recognized.
In Islamic thought, these correspond to ‘aqlī (rational) certainties.
3
Recognize the implication for idealism
If all knowledge were merely subjective mental constructions (as idealism holds), then any knowledge could be doubted. But the first type of knowledge is indubitable. Idealism cannot account for this absolute certainty.
The argument is that idealism makes no room for a category of knowledge that is necessarily true regardless of mental states.
4
Conclude the critique
Since idealism fails to distinguish between certain and fallible knowledge, it cannot adequately explain the structure of human knowledge. This is a weakness in the theory.
The argument does not refute idealism outright but shows it is incomplete.

Source Text

The Solipsism Argument Against Idealism
Author presents third argument against idealism: solipsism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how idealism leads to solipsism and why this consequence is absurd, especially in the context of Islamic discourse and debate.

Core Idea
If reality is only mental, other people are mere ideas — so who are we speaking to?

The third argument against idealism draws on a devastating implication: if everything is a mental representation, then other people have no objective existence. This leads to solipsism (the view that only one's own mind exists), which contradicts our everyday experience of communication and debate. We examine how this reduces idealism to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the idealist position
Idealism holds that all that exists are mental representations (‘tasawwurat dhimiyya wa fikriyya'). There is no external world independent of the mind.
This is the premise from which solipsism follows.
2
Recognize the consequence for other minds
If other people are perceived only as mental images, then they have no independent existence. They are just ideas in the perceiver's mind.
This is solipsism: only the self exists.
3
Identify the absurdity
The author asks: 'With whom do we speak? For whom do we debate and write?' If others are not real, then all communication is pointless. This is self-defeating because the idealist is engaging in discourse with others while denying their reality.
The act of arguing against someone presupposes their existence.
4
Conclude the refutation
Idealism leads to solipsism, which is absurd and pragmatically self-contradictory. Therefore, idealism must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Finally, the fifth argument (numbered 4) asks whether a person deprived of senses would have the same ideas as a normal person. The answer, whichever way, undermines idealism because it shows that sensory input shapes knowledge, implying an external source. The conclusion is that idealism is rejected by sound intellect and logic, as it abolishes objective knowledge.

Objective Reality and Sensory Deprivation Arguments
Author presents fourth argument about objective reality and sensory deprivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue that sensations are reactions to external stimuli and that sensory deprivation shows the dependence of knowledge on an external world, refuting idealism.

Core Idea
Without external reality, sensations are impossible, and sensory deprivation proves knowledge depends on the world.

The fourth argument against idealism comes in two parts. First, the author distinguishes between the objective reality of perceptions and their correspondence to our senses. Sensations are psychological reactions to external things, so an external world must cause them. Second, a thought experiment: a person who loses senses of sight and hearing would not have the same knowledge as a normal person, showing that knowledge is shaped by sensory input from an external world. Both points refute idealism.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish objective reality from subjective perception
The author separates the issue of existence of an objective reality (al-waqi' al-mawdu'i) from the issue of correspondence between that reality and our perceptions.
This distinction is critical to avoid confusion.
2
Understand sensations as reactions
Sensations are described as 'psychological reactions' (infi'alat nafsiyya) to external things. This implies there must be something external causing the reaction.
The argument is causal: effect (sensation) requires a cause (external object).
3
Consider the sensory deprivation thought experiment
The author asks: would the ideas of a blind and deaf person be complete and parallel to those of a normal person? The answer, either way, undermines idealism. If yes, then senses are irrelevant to knowledge, which is false. If no, then knowledge depends on senses, implying an external world affecting the senses.
This is a self-undermining dilemma for idealism.
4
Conclude against idealism
Both parts show that our knowledge is rooted in an external reality. Idealism, which denies objective reality, cannot account for the causal connection between world and sensations nor the role of the senses in shaping knowledge.
The author concludes that idealism is rejected by sound intellect.

And it is observed regarding this theory the following: 1) Professor Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr says that if human perceptions and knowledges have the property of self-revelation of a domain beyond their limits, then all sciences and knowledges must be correct because they are revealing by their nature and essence, and a thing does not abandon its essential description, while all thinkers of humanity acknowledge that many of the informations and judgments that people have are erroneous perceptions and do not reveal anything about reality. So how can it be understood in light of what this theory claims, that knowledge enjoys self-revelation? And does this philosophy have any escape except to abandon granting this attribute to knowledge?! 2) Human thinking possesses two types of knowledges: the first: knowledges guaranteed correct, which are the general foundation of thinking, absolutely indubitable, such as the principle of non-contradiction and the principle of existence and non-existence. The second: secondary knowledges deduced from the previous knowledges, which are where error occurs, as in the laws of physics and chemistry. This is the theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. It means that we do not acknowledge the existence of other people because they are nothing but mental and intellectual representations. So with whom do we speak? And for whom do we debate and write? 3) Objective reality and ideal reality: It is necessary to distinguish between two issues: one is the issue of the existence of an objective reality for perceptions and sensations, and the other is the correspondence of this reality to what appears to us in our perception and senses. If we distinguish between them, we can know that our sensations are merely psychological reactions to external things. So there must be something external when we sense and react, and we must question whether the existence of things in space outside the mind is a doubtful or false existence. Whoever doubts the existence of the world independent of us is undoubtedly insane. 4) Another question may be posed before idealism: Are the ideas of a person who has lost, for example, the senses of sight and hearing, complete, equal, and parallel to the ideas of a healthy person who has not lost these two senses? The answer to this question, whichever way it comes, demolishes idealist thinking from its foundation. The conclusion: This theory is not approved by sound intellect and logic, and it completely abolishes the issue of human knowledge and the study of its value from an objective perspective because it does not recognize the objectivity of thought and perception and the existence of anything outside their limits.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of idealist epistemology, which claims that knowledge is merely subjective and does not correspond to an external reality. The author presents four distinct arguments against this theory. The first argument, quoted from Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr, attacks the idealist notion that knowledge has 'self-revelation' (al-kashf al-dhati). If knowledge inherently reveals reality, then all knowledge would be correct. Yet we know that many human judgments are erroneous. Therefore, the idealist claim is self-contradictory; it must either abandon the attribute of self-revelation or concede that all knowledge is correct, which is false. The second argument points out that human thinking includes both certain, foundational principles (like the law of non-contradiction) and secondary, derived knowledge where error occurs. Idealism fails to account for this distinction. The third argument draws from the theory of knowledge between Quran and philosophy: idealism leads to solipsism, denying the existence of other minds. If everything is mental representations, then we have no basis for communication with others. The fourth argument concerns objective versus ideal reality. The author distinguishes between the existence of an objective reality for perceptions and the correspondence of that reality to our senses. Our sensations are reactions to external things, so there must be an external world. Doubting this is insanity. Finally, the fifth argument (numbered 4) asks whether a person deprived of senses would have the same ideas as a normal person. The answer, whichever way, undermines idealism because it shows that sensory input shapes knowledge, implying an external source. The conclusion is that idealism is rejected by sound intellect and logic, as it abolishes objective knowledge.

Two Objections to Psychoanalytic Reductionism
Two objections against Freudian theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two objections against Freudian reductionism and evaluate their logical structure using Islamic epistemological criteria.

Core Idea
Reducing all knowledge to instinct self-destructs: it cannot explain necessary truths or moral distinctions.

In this passage, the author raises two powerful objections against Freud's claim that all human behaviour is a distorted expression of repressed sexual instincts. These objections target the very foundations of knowledge and ethics. As students of Islamic logic, we learn to spot reductionist fallacies and to defend the objectivity of rational and moral truths. Let us examine each objection and see how they function as a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target theory
What is the claim being attacked? Freud says every human action, thought, belief — including science, philosophy, religion — is merely a disguised outlet for sexual instincts stored in the unconscious.
This is a strong reductionist thesis.
2
Extract the first objection: necessary truths
The author asks: if all is instinct, then what about self-evident truths like 2+2=4, the whole equals the sum of its parts, or that a straight line is the shortest distance? These are known without proof and cannot be explained as repressed desires.
Notice the appeal to 'badihiyyat' (self-evident axioms) — a key concept in Islamic logic.
3
Extract the second objection: moral values
If all actions are just distorted instincts, then there is no real difference between good and evil, trustworthy and treacherous. All moral distinctions vanish, making humans no different from animals.
This shows the ethical collapse of the theory.
4
Analyse the logical structure: reductio ad absurdum
Both objections work by showing that Freud's premise leads to absurd consequences: the denial of objective science and objective morality. Since these consequences are unacceptable, the premise must be false.
This is a valid logical move, similar to 'burhan al-tamanu' or 'burhan al-tadāfuq' in Islamic theology.
5
Connect to Islamic epistemological criteria
In the Islamic tradition, 'badihiyyat' are considered certain knowledge (yaqiniyyat). Moral values are known through reason and revelation (tahsin wa taqbih al-aqliyyayn). Freud's theory contradicts these foundations.
This shows the importance of a sound epistemology.

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against Freudian psychoanalytic theory. The first objection challenges the origin of necessary truths (البدهيات العقلية) such as mathematical laws (e.g., 2+2=4) and geometric axioms. The author argues that if all human behavior is reduced to sexual instincts, then these objective truths would also be reduced, leading to the negation of all sciences, philosophy, and religion. The second objection points to the moral consequences: if all actions are merely distorted expressions of the sexual instinct, then moral distinctions (good/evil, trustworthy/treacherous) collapse, negating human ethical values. Both objections aim to show the reductionist and self-defeating nature of the theory. The footnotes refer to the same source.

The Epistemological Gap in Freud's Theory
Author highlights Freud's failure to address moral distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological omission in Freud's theory that it does not account for how humans distinguish between moral opposites.

Core Idea
Freud explains instincts but not how we judge truth from falsehood.

In this passage, the author points out a fundamental flaw: Freud's theory of latent instincts never addresses one of the most basic human capacities—distinguishing between truth and falsehood, right and wrong. This is not a minor oversight; it is an epistemological gap that undermines the theory's claim to explain human knowledge. Let's examine this critique closely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unasked question
The author states that the theory never asks how humans distinguish between contradictory things. List the pairs mentioned: truth/falsehood, piety/immorality, right/wrong, beauty/ugliness.
These are fundamental epistemological distinctions.
2
Understand the implication
By ignoring this, the theory fails to account for the basis of moral and intellectual judgment. It reduces all human behavior to instincts without explaining the rational capacity to evaluate.
This highlights a limitation of purely instinct-based explanations.
3
Recognize the author's strategy
The author uses this omission as a critique: a theory of human behavior that cannot address these distinctions is incomplete. The burden is on the theory to explain or be rejected.
This is a common critical move in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤) ان نظرية الغرائز الكامنة هذه لم تتطرق البتة الى السؤال المهم وهو : كيف يستطيع الانسان ان يميز بين الاشياء المتناقضة . الحق والباطل . التقوى والفجور . الصواب والخطأ . الجمال والقبح . (٥) لو قلنا لفرويد ان نظريتك في الغرائز الضامرة صحيحة سنصل الى نتيجة : ان نظرية التحليل النفسي هي تعبير عن غرائزك الجنسية المكبوتة الخاصة بك ياسيد فرويد والضامرة في اعماق لاشعورك ومن ثم لا يمكن تعميمها لجميع الاشخاص ولجميع الازمنة والجميع الحالات وهي لا تعني الناس الآخرين من بعيد أو قريب (٦) ان مفهوم اللذة لا يقتصر على اللذة الجنسية فقط فهناك لذة الطعام ولذة النظر ولذة المطالعة فماهو المقياس الذي يختار من خلاله الانسان هذه اللذة او تلك اليس هو العقل . وقد اشار الامام علي بن ابي طالب - ع - الى هذا الامر بقوله (ما أضمر أحد شيئا الا ظهر في فلتات لسانه وصفحات وجهه) ولكن ان تنسب المعرفة الانسانية الى هذه المكبوتات ففيه مغالاة وتجن على الحقيقة الواقعية المنظورة.
(4) This theory of latent instincts never addressed the important question: How can man distinguish between contradictory things? Truth and falsehood, piety and immorality, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness. (5) If we said to Freud that your theory of repressed instincts is correct, we would reach the conclusion that the theory of psychoanalysis is an expression of your own repressed sexual instincts, O Mr. Freud, which are latent in the depths of your unconscious, and therefore it cannot be generalized to all persons, all times, and all states, and it does not concern other people at all. (6) The concept of pleasure is not limited to sexual pleasure alone; there is the pleasure of food, the pleasure of sight, and the pleasure of reading. What is the criterion by which man chooses this pleasure or that? Is it not reason? Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib (peace be upon him) pointed to this matter when he said: 'No one conceals anything but it appears in the slips of his tongue and the expressions of his face.' But to attribute human knowledge to these repressions is an exaggeration and an injustice to observable reality.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of Freudian theory of instincts from an epistemological perspective. The author first (point 4) notes that the theory fails to address how humans distinguish between opposites like truth and falsehood, piety and immorality, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness. These are fundamental epistemological distinctions that any theory of knowledge must account for. Then (point 5) the author deploys a self-refutation argument: if Freud's theory is true, then Freud's own theory is merely an expression of his personal repressed sexual instincts, and thus cannot claim universality. This is a classic method of refutation (naqd) in Islamic logic. Next (point 6), the author argues that pleasure is not exclusively sexual; there are multiple kinds of pleasure (food, sight, reading), and it is reason that chooses among them, not unconscious instincts. The author then cites Imam Ali's dictum that hidden things appear in slips of tongue and facial expressions, but warns against overextending this to attribute all human knowledge to repressions. The critique uses rational argument and an authoritative quote from the Islamic tradition to challenge the sufficiency of Freud's theory.

Self-Refutation: Freud's Theory Undermines Itself
Author uses self-refutation argument against Freud's theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a self-refutation argument against universal claims using conditional logic.

Core Idea
If Freud's theory is true, it applies only to Freud—it refutes its own universality.

The author now uses a powerful logical move: turning the theory against itself. If Freud's theory were correct, then his own psychoanalysis would be merely an expression of his personal repressed instincts, and therefore not universally valid. This is a classic self-refutation argument similar to the liar paradox. Let's break down the logic.

Guided Path
1
Formulate the conditional premise
The author says: 'If we said to Freud that your theory of latent instincts is correct, we would reach the conclusion that psychoanalysis is an expression of your own repressed sexual instincts.' So: If theory is true, then psychoanalysis is Freud's personal expression.
This is the antecedent-consequent relationship.
2
State the implication
If psychoanalysis is just Freud's personal expression, then it cannot be generalized to all people, times, and states. So the theory claims universality, but the consequence denies it.
This creates a contradiction with the theory's own claim of universal applicability.
3
Derive the conclusion
Therefore, the theory cannot be true in the sense it claims, or it is self-refuting. The author concludes that it cannot be generalized.
The argument is logically valid: if A then B, and B is false (or contradicts A), then A is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٤) ان نظرية الغرائز الكامنة هذه لم تتطرق البتة الى السؤال المهم وهو : كيف يستطيع الانسان ان يميز بين الاشياء المتناقضة . الحق والباطل . التقوى والفجور . الصواب والخطأ . الجمال والقبح . (٥) لو قلنا لفرويد ان نظريتك في الغرائز الضامرة صحيحة سنصل الى نتيجة : ان نظرية التحليل النفسي هي تعبير عن غرائزك الجنسية المكبوتة الخاصة بك ياسيد فرويد والضامرة في اعماق لاشعورك ومن ثم لا يمكن تعميمها لجميع الاشخاص ولجميع الازمنة والجميع الحالات وهي لا تعني الناس الآخرين من بعيد أو قريب (٦) ان مفهوم اللذة لا يقتصر على اللذة الجنسية فقط فهناك لذة الطعام ولذة النظر ولذة المطالعة فماهو المقياس الذي يختار من خلاله الانسان هذه اللذة او تلك اليس هو العقل . وقد اشار الامام علي بن ابي طالب - ع - الى هذا الامر بقوله (ما أضمر أحد شيئا الا ظهر في فلتات لسانه وصفحات وجهه) ولكن ان تنسب المعرفة الانسانية الى هذه المكبوتات ففيه مغالاة وتجن على الحقيقة الواقعية المنظورة.
(4) This theory of latent instincts never addressed the important question: How can man distinguish between contradictory things? Truth and falsehood, piety and immorality, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness. (5) If we said to Freud that your theory of repressed instincts is correct, we would reach the conclusion that the theory of psychoanalysis is an expression of your own repressed sexual instincts, O Mr. Freud, which are latent in the depths of your unconscious, and therefore it cannot be generalized to all persons, all times, and all states, and it does not concern other people at all. (6) The concept of pleasure is not limited to sexual pleasure alone; there is the pleasure of food, the pleasure of sight, and the pleasure of reading. What is the criterion by which man chooses this pleasure or that? Is it not reason? Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib (peace be upon him) pointed to this matter when he said: 'No one conceals anything but it appears in the slips of his tongue and the expressions of his face.' But to attribute human knowledge to these repressions is an exaggeration and an injustice to observable reality.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of Freudian theory of instincts from an epistemological perspective. The author first (point 4) notes that the theory fails to address how humans distinguish between opposites like truth and falsehood, piety and immorality, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness. These are fundamental epistemological distinctions that any theory of knowledge must account for. Then (point 5) the author deploys a self-refutation argument: if Freud's theory is true, then Freud's own theory is merely an expression of his personal repressed sexual instincts, and thus cannot claim universality. This is a classic method of refutation (naqd) in Islamic logic. Next (point 6), the author argues that pleasure is not exclusively sexual; there are multiple kinds of pleasure (food, sight, reading), and it is reason that chooses among them, not unconscious instincts. The author then cites Imam Ali's dictum that hidden things appear in slips of tongue and facial expressions, but warns against overextending this to attribute all human knowledge to repressions. The critique uses rational argument and an authoritative quote from the Islamic tradition to challenge the sufficiency of Freud's theory.

Key Terms
نظرية الغرائز الضامرة
نتيجة
تعميم
لاشعور
مكبوتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the self-refutation argument, what does the author conclude about Freud's theory?
A) It is true but incomplete.
B) It cannot be generalized because it is an expression of Freud's personal instincts.
C) It explains all human behavior perfectly.
D) It is valid for some cultures but not others.

Key Takeaway
A theory that claims universality but self-destructs when applied to its own originator is logically unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a self-refutation argument against the claim: 'All knowledge is subjective and cannot be universal.' Show that the claim itself, if true, would be subjective and thus not universally binding.
?
In kalam, the claim that 'everything is created by God' can be tested: use self-refutation to see if the claim itself is created by God, and what that implies. Formulate the argument.

Reason as the Criterion for Pleasures
Author argues reason, not instinct, is the criterion for choosing pleasures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why reason, rather than instinct, must be the criterion for evaluating pleasures according to the author.

Core Idea
Pleasure is diverse; reason chooses, not unconscious instincts.

The author now broadens the critique: pleasure is not exclusively sexual. There are many kinds—physical, aesthetic, intellectual. Choosing among them requires a criterion, and that criterion is reason ('aql). The author cites Imam Ali's maxim about hidden things appearing externally, but warns against overextending it. Let's analyze this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List the different pleasures mentioned
The author mentions pleasure of food (ladhdhat al-ta'am), pleasure of sight (al-nazar), and pleasure of reading (al-mutala'a). These are non-reproductive pleasures.
This shows pleasure is not unified under a single instinct.
2
Pose the criterion question
The author asks: 'What is the criterion by which man chooses this pleasure or that?' The implied answer is reason (al-'aql).
Reason evaluates and prioritizes pleasures.
3
Introduce the Islamic evidence
The author quotes Imam Ali: 'No one conceals anything but it appears in the slips of his tongue and the expressions of his face.' This supports that inner states manifest externally, but the author warns against reducing all knowledge to repressions.
The quote validates observation of inner states, but not the exclusivity of Freud's theory.
4
Identify the author's conclusion
Attributing all human knowledge to repressions is an exaggeration (mughalat) and an injustice (tajan) to observable reality.
The author upholds reason and empirical reality as checks.

The Two Factors in the Relativist Theory of Knowledge
Author outlines the relativist theory with two factors influencing mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two factors that, according to the relativist theory, cooperate to produce mental images.

Core Idea
Perception blends object and subject; two factors shape mental images.

In this passage, the author presents the relativist theory of knowledge, attributing it to Paul Faurie. The theory argues that what we perceive is not pure reality but a mixture of objective and subjective elements. Two main factors are identified: the spatiotemporal context and the nervous system. Understanding these factors is crucial to grasping the relativist position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosopher and the theory
Note the philosopher Paul Faurie and the general claim that perception is a blend of objective and subjective aspects.
The philosopher's name may be unfamiliar; focus on the theory's content.
2
Locate the two factors
In the Arabic text, find the enumeration of factors: (1) temporal and spatial circumstances, (2) the nervous system. The text explicitly states there are two main factors.
Read carefully the sentences starting with 'هناك عاملان رئيسان'.
3
Examine the examples
The text gives examples: a person appearing beautiful in one context but not another, and food tasting different in health vs illness. These illustrate how each factor influences perception.
Consider how the same external object can produce different mental images due to these factors.
4
Understand how they cooperate
The factors jointly produce the mental image; they do not operate independently. The image is a product of both objective and subjective elements.
The phrase 'يشتركان في اخراج الصورة الذهنية' emphasizes cooperation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعد (الفيلسوف بول فورییه ۱۷۷۲ - ۱۸۳۷ م ) (واحدا من ابرز رواد هذه النظرية ) ( ان الخطوط العامة لهذه النظرية يمكن اجمالها بالآتيان ما يدركه الانسان ليس حقيقة خالصة من الشوائب بل هي مزيج من الناحية الموضوعية للشيء الخارج كالمنظر الطبيعي - والناحية الذاتية للفكر المدرك - طبيعة شخصية الفرد فهناك عاملان رئيسان يشتركان في اخراج الصورة الذهنية وهما (١ الظروف الزمانية والمكانية المحيطة بالمدرك فهي تسهم في بلورة الاشياء مختلفة إذ نرى شخصا معينا يتجلى في ظرف خاص جميلا وفي ظرف آخر غير جميل. وما هذا الا لأن للظروف تأثيرها في اظهار كيفيات الاشياء . الجهاز العصبي . له دور جوهري في تبلور الصور العلمية فالاشياء الخارجية تظهر لدى مدارك الانسان بألوان مختلفة فالشخص يدرك الشيء الواحد في حالات مختلف فنرى الانسان في سلامته يلتذ بالطعام دون حالة سقمه والرائحة تكون طيبة له في حالة دون أخرى وغير ذلك فالصور الذهنية ليست سوى اشارات الى الخارج وليست كاشفة عنه كشفا تاما لان كيفياتها متوقفة على مدى تأثير العاملين آنفي الذكر فيها. ان جميع الحقائق ذات طابع نسبي حسب الدور الذي يلعبه حس كل فرد وعقله في عملية ادراكه للحقائق ولما كانت الظروف والشروط تختلف في الاشخاص والحالات المتنوعة كانت
The philosopher Paul Faurie (1772-1837 CE) is considered one of the most prominent pioneers of this theory. The general lines of this theory can be summarized as follows: What man perceives is not a pure reality free of impurities, but rather a mixture of the objective aspect of the external thing — like a natural scene — and the subjective aspect of the perceiving mind — the personal nature of the individual. There are two main factors that cooperate in producing the mental image: (1) The temporal and spatial circumstances surrounding the perceiver; they contribute to shaping things differently, as we see a particular person appearing beautiful in one context and not beautiful in another. This is only because circumstances have an effect in manifesting the qualities of things. (2) The nervous system has a fundamental role in shaping mental images. External things appear to human perceptions in different colors. A person perceives the same thing in different states; we see a person in health enjoying food, unlike in illness, and a smell is pleasant in one state and not in another, etc. Thus mental images are merely indications of the external world and not complete disclosures of it, because their qualities depend on the extent of the influence of the two aforementioned factors in them. All truths are relative in nature, according to the role played by each individual's sense and intellect in the process of perceiving truths. And since circumstances and conditions differ among persons and varied states, they were...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the relativist theory of knowledge, attributed to Paul Faurie. The author explains that human perception is a blend of objective and subjective factors. Two main factors are identified: first, the spatiotemporal context of the perceiver, which influences how things appear (e.g., a person may look beautiful in one situation but not another). Second, the nervous system, which shapes mental images differently depending on the state of the perceiver (e.g., enjoying food when healthy, disgust when ill). The conclusion is that mental images are only signs pointing to external reality, not complete representations. Therefore, all truths are relative to the individual's sense and reason and the prevailing conditions. This is a concise exposition of epistemological relativism.

Key Terms
النظرية النسبية (al-nazariyya al-nisbiyya): The theory that knowledge is relative, depending on subjective and objective factors; no absolute truth is attainable.
الصورة الذهنية (al-sura al-dhihniyya): Mental image; the result of perception, which is a mixture of external object and subjective conditions.
الجهاز العصبي (al-jihaz al-'asabi): The nervous system; a key factor in shaping perceptions, causing variation in how external things appear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relativist theory presented, what two factors cooperate to produce mental images?
A) Reason and revelation
B) Temporal/spatial circumstances and the nervous system
C) Senses and intellect
D) Object and subject

Key Takeaway
The relativist theory holds that mental images result from both objective external things and subjective internal conditions, particularly spatiotemporal context and the nervous system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a scenario in Islamic jurisprudence where two scholars derive different rulings from the same evidence. Using the relativist framework, identify which subjective factors (e.g., local custom, personal experience) might have influenced their mental images of the evidence.
