Refuting Dialectical Materialism from Islamic Epistemology
Author enumerates three principles of dialectical materialism with an agricultural example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three principles of dialectical materialism and refute each using Islamic epistemological arguments.

Core Idea
Dialectical materialism denies the divine origin of knowledge; Islam affirms it.

The passage presents the dialectical materialist theory of knowledge, which claims that thought is merely a reflection of matter. As students of the Islamic intellectual tradition, you must be able to recognize this worldview and critically evaluate it using the tools of Islamic epistemology. This lesson guides you through the core principles and shows how they contradict the Islamic understanding of knowledge and existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
Locate the three enumerated principles in the passage: (1) matter is in constant change, (2) change arises from internal contradiction (negative vs positive) producing a synthesis, (3) economic class struggle drives history. Write them down in your own words.
2
Analyze the wheat example
Examine the wheat example: a grain placed in soil undergoes a struggle between negation and survival, resulting in a plant. Ask: Does this example prove that internal contradiction is the cause of change, or is it a metaphor imposed on nature?
The example assumes that the grain has a 'will' to survive, which implies teleology – contrary to strict materialism.
3
Compare materialist and Islamic epistemology
The passage claims thought is a reflection of matter; the mind does not impose on reality. Contrast with the Islamic view: knowledge comes from Allah, and the intellect (aql) is a faculty given by God to discern truth. Use Quran 16:78: 'And Allah has brought you out from the wombs of your mothers while you know nothing, and He made for you hearing, vision, and intellect.'
4
Construct a refutation
Using Islamic premises, argue that materialism is self-refuting: if all thought is determined by matter, then the theory itself is merely a product of material conditions, not necessarily true. Then cite revelation to affirm that the universe was created by God and that knowledge originates from Him.
This step requires synthesizing rational and scriptural arguments.

Source Text

(١ ان المادة وكل ما في الكون في حالة تكامل و تبدل وتغير مستمرين . ۲ ) ان هذا التبدل أو التغير ينشأ نتيجة لصراع داخلي في جوهر الاشياء بين جانب سلبي وجانب ايجابي ثم يتولد من هذا الصراع شيء ثالث هو الصورة المتكاملة للشيء . فحبة الحنطة مثلا اذا وضعت في التراب وسقيت بالماء ينشأ في صميم ذاتها صراع بين ما يريد نفي وجودها وما يريد ثباتها وبقائها فيتولد من هذا الصراع تفتح الحبة ثم نموها واخضرارها ولا تزال تتطور في ظل هذا الصراع حتى تصير نبتة متكاملة
٣ ) ان الصراع الاقتصادي بين طبقتي المستغلين والمستغلين يعتبر المحرك الاول والعامل الاساس الحاسم في حركة التاريخ .
فالعلاقات بين ظواهر الطبيعة متداخلة مترابطة ارتباطا عضويا وثيقا وليس حركة الفكر الا انعكاسا لحركة الواقع منقولة ومحولة في مخ الانسان فالتفكير عبارة عن الاثر المتولد من الطبيعة في الاعصاب والدماغ. ان تفاعل المادة الخارجية المتحركة المتطورة مع المادة الدماغية ينتج منهما العلم والتفكير. والادراك هو اثر هذا التفاعل فالعقل لايملي اوامره على الواقع بل ان الواقع الخارجي هو الذي يملي اوامره على العقل . قال كارل ماركس (لا يمكن فصل الفكر عن المادة المفكرة. فان هذه المادة هي جوهر كل التغيرات " وقال انجلز ان) شعورنا وفكرنا مهما ظهرا لنا متعاليين ليس سوى نتاج عضو مادي
Theory of reflection of the objective world, known as the dialectical materialist theory of knowledge, adopted by the German philosopher Karl Marx (1818-1883) and his friend Friedrich Engels (1820-1895). The essence of their theory is that nothing exists in the universe except matter and its forces; existence is matter. Man is part of nature, so his life must be governed by what governs all of nature: laws and norms from the nature of things. The most important foundations of this theory are: (1) Matter and everything in the universe is in a state of continuous integration, change, and transformation. (2) This change or transformation arises as a result of an internal struggle in the essence of things between a negative side and a positive side, then from this struggle a third thing is generated, which is the complete image of the thing. For example, a grain of wheat: when placed in soil and watered, a struggle arises in its core between what wants to negate its existence and what wants its stability and survival. From this struggle emerges the splitting of the grain, then its growth and greening, and it continues to develop under this struggle until it becomes a complete plant. (3) The economic struggle between the exploiting and exploited classes is considered the primary driver and the decisive fundamental factor in the movement of history. The relations between natural phenomena are interconnected in a tight organic relationship. Thought is nothing but a reflection of the movement of reality, transferred and transformed in the human brain. Thinking is the effect generated from nature in the nerves and brain. The interaction of external moving developing matter with brain matter produces knowledge and thinking. Perception is the effect of this interaction. The mind does not impose its commands on reality; rather, external reality imposes its commands on the mind. Karl Marx said: 'Thought cannot be separated from thinking matter. This matter is the essence of all changes.' And Engels said: 'Our consciousness and thought, no matter how transcendent they appear to us, are nothing but the product of a material organ.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the dialectical materialist theory of knowledge (al-madiyya al-jadaliyya). It begins by stating that only matter exists and man is part of nature subject to natural laws. Three principles are enumerated: (1) matter is in constant change, (2) change arises from internal contradiction (negative vs positive) producing a new synthesis — exemplified by a grain of wheat that struggles and grows into a plant, (3) class struggle drives history. Then it explains that thought is a reflection of material reality in the brain; perception results from the interaction of external matter with brain matter. The mind does not impose on reality; reality imposes on the mind. Quotes from Marx and Engels reinforce that thought is a product of matter. From an Islamic perspective, this contradicts the belief that knowledge comes from Allah and that the human soul is immaterial. For example, the Quran says 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96), attributing creation and knowledge to God, not matter.

صراع

Key Takeaway
Dialectical materialism's reduction of thought to matter contradicts Islamic teachings on the divine origin of knowledge and the immaterial soul, and can be refuted using both rational and scriptural arguments.

Distinguishing Mechanical and Dialectical Materialism
Author traces materialism to Democritus' atomic theory and contrasts it with dialectical materialism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mechanical materialism and dialectical materialism based on their views of motion and causation.

Core Idea
The source of motion—external or internal—is the key difference between two materialist philosophies.

This passage traces materialism back to Democritus and his atomic theory, which explains all phenomena through the mechanical movement of atoms. It then introduces the Marxist alternative where motion is inherent. Understanding this contrast helps you evaluate materialist claims from an Islamic perspective, where atomism (juz' la yatajazz') is used for theistic purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two materialist positions
Locate in the passage the description of Democritus' atomism (mechanical materialism) and the later Marx/Engels position (dialectical materialism). Note the names and key features attributed to each.
Democritus lived in 5th century BCE; Marx and Engels in 19th century CE.
2
Compare views on origin of motion
Focus on the Arabic phrases: for Democritus, motion is 'مفروضة عليها' (imposed), while for Marx it is 'ذاتي نابع من صميمها' (inherent, arising from its essence). This is the pivotal difference.
Mechanical materialism sees motion as externally caused; dialectical materialism sees it as internal and self-generating.
3
Compare epistemologies
Notice how Democritus explains knowledge: senses transmit external images to the mind (passive reception). Contrast with the implied epistemology of dialectical materialism, where motion is internal—knowledge may also be seen as active engagement.
The passage does not elaborate Marx's epistemology, only the nature of motion.
4
Relate to Islamic atomism
Using the commentary, understand that Islamic mutakallimun (like al-Ash'ari) adopted atomism but with a crucial difference: atoms are created by God at every instant, and motion is directly from God, not autonomous. This contrasts with both materialisms.
Islamic atomism is theistic, while Democritus' is atheistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان اول من قال بالفلسفة المادية هو الفليلسوف اليوناني ديمقريطس الذي عاش في القرن الخامس قبل الميلاد . وتقول نظريته الذرية ان المادة عبارة عن جزيئات صغيرة صلبة لا تقبل التغير والانقسام - جوهر - اما الظواهر الطبيعية كالانسان والحيوان والنبات فهي ناتجة من انتقال تلك الجواهر من مكان الى مكان وكل حركات المادة مفروضة عليها وليس نابعة من ذاتها وصميمها، فالريح تحرك الهواء والهواء يحرك الماء والماء يحرك النبات وان ادوات الحس تنقل الخارج الى الذهن فالعين تنقل صورة الاشياء الى الذهن والاذن تنقل الصوت وهكذا وقد عرف هذا المنظور بالاتجاه المادي الميكانيكي وعلى ضوء هذا المنهج وباتجاه مغاير قال ماركس وانجلز نظريتهم في الخلق و المعرفة فقالا ان الحركة في المادة امر ذاتي نابع من صميمها ولا تفرض من عامل خارج عنها
The first to propose materialist philosophy was the Greek philosopher Democritus who lived in the fifth century BCE. His atomic theory says that matter consists of small solid particles that do not accept change or division - substance. As for natural phenomena like humans, animals, and plants, they result from the transfer of those substances from one place to another. All movements of matter are imposed upon it, not arising from its own essence. So wind moves air, air moves water, water moves plants, and the senses transmit the external to the mind: the eye transmits the image of things to the mind, the ear transmits sound, etc. This perspective was known as the mechanical materialist trend. In the light of this method and in a contradictory direction, Marx and Engels stated their theory of creation and knowledge, saying that motion in matter is an internal matter arising from its essence and is not imposed by an external factor.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage provides a historical background to materialism, crediting Democritus as the first materialist. Democritus' atomism posits that matter consists of indivisible solid particles (atoms) that move by external forces (wind, etc.), and all phenomena result from their combination. This is 'mechanical materialism'. In contrast, Marx and Engels proposed 'dialectical materialism' where motion is inherent in matter, not imposed externally. In Islamic thought, atomism was used by the mutakallimun (e.g., al-Ash'ari) to argue for God's constant creation, as atoms are accidents needing a Creator. Here, Democritus' atomism leads to atheism, which is rejected in Islam.

Key Terms
ذرية (dhariyya): Atomism: the theory that matter is composed of small, solid, indivisible particles. [Note: In Islamic theology (kalam), atomism is used to prove the temporal origin of the world and the existence of God; here it is used in a materialist context.]
مادة ميكانيكية (madda mikaniyya): Mechanical materialism: the view that matter moves only by external forces. [Note: This term is modern; classical Islamic philosophy discusses hylomorphism, not materialism.]
جوهر (jawhar): Substance: here referring to the indivisible atoms of Democritus. [Note: In Islamic philosophy, jawhar often means substance (ousia); in atomism it means the atom itself.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes Marx and Engels' materialism from Democritus'?
A) Democritus believed in God
B) Marx and Engels believed motion is internal to matter
C) Democritus thought matter is spiritual
D) Marx and Engels rejected atomism

Key Takeaway
The passage's essential point is that the nature of motion—external (mechanical) or internal (dialectical)—is the key distinction between Democritean and Marxist materialism, and Islamic kalam offers a third view where motion is from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting Materialist Brain Theory via Mullā Ṣadrā
Author cites Mullā Ṣadrā to refute materialist claims about the brain's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Mullā Ṣadrā's reductio argument against physicalist theories of mind and identify where the fallacy of 'imprinting the large in the small' lies.

Core Idea
If vast concepts were physically in the brain, the large would be imprinted in the small – an impossibility.

This passage presents a powerful metaphysical critique of materialism drawn from the Illuminationist tradition. By closely examining Mullā Ṣadrā's argument, we will see how he exposes the incoherence of locating immaterial concepts in a finite physical organ. The argument hinges on the absurdity of the 'large imprinting in the small,' leading to the conclusion that the brain is not a container but a means for the soul to perceive in a non-material realm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The materialist claim is that concepts like towering mountains and great planets are physically located in the frontal lobe of the brain (muqaddam al-dimāgh).
2
Extract the conditional reasoning
Mullā Ṣadrā sets up a conditional: If those concepts were physically in the frontal lobe, then the large would be imprinted in the small (inṭibāʿ al-ʿaẓīm fī al-ṣaghīr).
This conditional is the core of the reductio.
3
Recognize the reductio ad absurdum
The consequent (large imprinted in small) is impossible ('mā lā yakhfā buṭlānuhu'). Therefore, the antecedent must be false: concepts are not physically in the brain.
This is modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
4
Understand the alternative explanation
Mullā Ṣadrā proposes that the frontal lobe is not a container but a 'prepared manifestation' (muzhir) for the soul to observe images in the greater imaginal realm (ʿālam al-mithāl al-aʿẓam).
This shifts the locus of perception from the physical brain to the immaterial soul.
5
Address the computer analogy objection
The author anticipates an objection: computers store millions of images. The counter is that computer images are devoid of perception (khāliya ʿan al-idrāk), whereas human concepts involve conscious awareness.
The analogy fails because it conflates storage with conscious perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the place of these things were the frontal lobe of the brain, then those things would have to occur in it, and the imprinting of the large in the small is obviously false. Hence, the frontal lobe is a prepared manifestation for the soul to observe those images and forms in the greater imaginal realm. And if you say that millions of images are stored in the computer, these images are devoid of perception.

Commentary
This passage presents a metaphysical argument against materialism using the principle that the larger cannot be physically contained in the smaller. The author quotes Mullā Ṣadrā: we can conceive of huge mountains and planets; if these concepts were physically located in the brain's frontal lobe, the large would have to be imprinted in the small—an impossibility. Therefore, the brain is not the place of these ideas but rather a means for the soul to perceive images in the 'greater imaginal realm' (ʿālam al-mithāl al-aʿẓam). The author anticipates an objection about computer storage, but counters that computer images have no perception. This argument aligns with the Islamic philosophical position on the immateriality of the soul and the intermediary world of images (barzakh).

The Argument from the Constancy of Thought
Author argues that the constancy of thought despite continuous material change proves the mind is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument from constancy of thought against materialism and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
If thought were material, memory would be impossible; since memory exists, the mind is immaterial.

In this lesson, we dissect a key philosophical argument used by Islamic thinkers to refute materialist accounts of the mind. The author points to the stability of memory and self-awareness despite the constant flux of matter. By parsing the reasoning, you will learn how this argument functions as a proof for the immateriality of the soul (rūḥ).

Guided Path
1
Identify the materialist claim under attack
Locate the assertion by Marxism and biologists that change is a fundamental property of matter, implying everything material is in constant flux.
2
Extract the key premise about matter
Find the sentence stating that change and instability are among the most prominent properties of matter. This serves as the major premise.
The author uses cells changing millions of times per second as evidence.
3
Find the observation about thought's constancy
Identify where the author asserts that thought and consciousness remain stable over a lifetime, contradicting what would be expected if they were material.
4
Reconstruct the logical form
Frame the argument as a conditional: If thought were material, it would be subject to constant change. But thought is constant. Therefore, thought is not material. This is modus tollens.
The memory argument reinforces this: if thought were material, forgetfulness would occur every moment.
5
Evaluate the strength of the argument
Consider whether the premise 'matter always changes' is universally true. Also, note that the author presents memory as a challenge that materialism cannot explain, strengthening the case for immateriality.
Think about potential objections: Could memory be explained by stable patterns in neural networks?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ التغير ان التغير وعدم الثبات من ابرز خواص المادة كما تؤمن الماركسية ويقول علماء الاحياء ان الخلايا تتغير ملايين المرات في الثانية الواحدة فلماذا يكون الفكر ثابتا ؟ اليس هذا دليلا على تجرد الفكر عن الدماغ ؟ ولو كان الفكر جزء من المادة لحدث النسيان في كل لحظة لجميع المعلومات السابقة. واذا رجع الانسان الى ما مضى من حياته يجد ان شعوره ثابت بذاته لم يتغير بمرور السنين وتبدل الظروف والاحوال منذ الطفولة حتى الكهولة ان الذاكرة هي السؤال الاهم الذي تهربت النظرية الماركسية الجدلية من التطرق اليه وحيرها فهي عاجزة امام هذا السؤال الكبير؟!
3. Change: Change and instability are among the most prominent properties of matter, as Marxism believes, and biologists say that cells change millions of times per second. So why is thought constant? Is this not evidence of thought's abstraction from the brain? If thought were part of matter, forgetfulness would occur every moment for all previous information. When a person looks back at his past life, he finds that his consciousness is constant in itself, unchanged by the passage of years and the change of circumstances from childhood to old age. Memory is the most important question that dialectical Marxist theory has evaded and baffled it; it is impotent before this great question?!
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage critiques materialism by highlighting the constancy of thought and memory despite continuous material change. The author notes that change is a fundamental property of matter—cells change millions of times per second. If thought were material, it would be in constant flux, leading to complete forgetfulness. Yet, consciousness remains stable over a lifetime. Memory, which requires stability, is a problem that dialectical materialism cannot explain. This argument parallels the Islamic philosophical proof for the immaterial soul (rūḥ) based on the unity and continuity of self-awareness.

Constancy of Laws and Absolute Truths
Author argues that dialectical materialists contradict themselves and lists constant truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the self-contradiction in the dialectical materialist's position on absolute truths and recognise examples of immutable truths in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Admitting any fixed truth refutes the claim that all truth is relative.

The author presents a powerful critique: if dialectical materialists treat their own claims as absolute, they undermine their relativism. Then he lists examples of truths that have not changed for centuries — mathematical laws, ethical principles, rational axioms. These are not subject to dialectical evolution. This lesson helps you see how the Islamic tradition affirms certain fixed truths, such as the prohibition of intoxicants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical materialist's claim
Read the opening line: the author assumes the dialectical materialist says something is an 'absolute fixed truth'. What is the materialist's usual position on truth? They deny absolute truths. So this is a contradiction.
The text says 'if they said it is an absolute fixed truth, then they have admitted the existence of absolute fixed truths'.
2
List the examples of constant truths
The author enumerates four categories: mathematical laws (e.g., 2+2=4), ethical principles (e.g., love of good and hatred of evil), psychical phenomena, and rational axioms (e.g., the whole is greater than the part). These have remained unchanged.
In Islamic thought, ethical principles like 'justice is good' are also considered fixed.
3
Connect to Islamic sciences
Consider the prohibition of intoxicants (khamr). The ruling is absolute and not subject to change, even if material conditions change. This parallels the author's point about ethical truths.
Many usuli principles (e.g., 'certainty is not overruled by doubt') are also constant.
4
Reflect on the significance
The author uses this to argue against the complete relativism of dialectical materialism. In Islamic epistemology, some truths are necessary (daruriyyat) and universal.

Commentary
This passage begins with a brief argument that the dialectical materialists, in claiming something as an absolute fixed truth, have contradicted their own relativism. The author then presents the fifth criticism: the constancy of laws. He identifies four domains of unchanging truths: mathematical laws (e.g., 2+2=4), ethical principles (e.g., love of good and hatred of evil), psychical phenomena, and rational axioms (e.g., the whole is greater than the part). These have remained unchanged for centuries and are not subject to dialectical evolution. The number (422) is unclear but perhaps indicates another example. Thus, the author refutes the claim that all knowledge is relative and historically determined. In Islamic thought, the prohibition of intoxicants (khamr) is an unchanging ethical ruling: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.'

Why Scientific Experiment Cannot Be the Ultimate Criterion
Author argues that experiment itself relies on rational axioms, so it cannot be the ultimate criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rational axioms underlying scientific experiment and explain why experiment cannot be the ultimate criterion of truth according to this critique.

Core Idea
Scientific experiment is not self-sufficient; it depends on rational axioms like causality that are beyond sensory perception.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful critique of the dialectical materialist criterion of truth: scientific experiment. He argues that experiment is not purely empirical but relies on rational principles that themselves are not sensed. This challenges the very foundation of empiricism. Let us dissect this argument and understand its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find where the author states the claim of the dialectical materialists: that scientific experiment is the criterion for distinguishing truth from error.
The claim is introduced in the first sentence.
2
Extract the author's objection
The author objects that this criterion is 'sterile' because experiment itself is not perceived by the senses but by primary rational axioms. Highlight the key concepts mentioned: cause and effect, necessity, possibility.
These are a priori rational concepts, not empirical.
3
Analyze the supporting analogy
The author notes that animals have the same sensory apparatus as humans yet lack rational understanding. What does this show about the sufficiency of sense perception?
It shows that senses alone are insufficient for knowledge.
4
Assess the scope of the critique
The author lists oppositions (truth/falsehood, piety/immorality, love/hatred) that cannot be distinguished by experiment. Why are these problematic for dialectical materialism?
They are value judgments and abstract concepts, not empirical facts.
5
Evaluate the internal validity
Consider whether the author's own argument relies on rational axioms. Does the critique itself use reason to argue against reason? How does this affect the critique?
It is coherent: the critique uses rational analysis to show the limits of empiricism, which is a valid meta-epistemological move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ المعيار ماهو معيار تشخيص الخطأ والصواب في المذهب الجدلي اليس هو التجربة العلمية . الا ان هذا المعيار عقيم تماما لان التجربة نفسها لاتدرك بالحس بل بالاسس العقلية الاولية البدهية . ان مفاهيم رئيسة لا يمكن ادراكها بالحس المجرد مثل العلة والمعلول والضرورة والامكان ولا يمكن لاي جهاز ان يدركها ولاننسى التذكير بان الحيوان يمتلك الاجهزة الحسية نفسها التي يمتلكها الانسان كذلك فان هذا المفهوم ينطبق على ماهية التمييز بين الاضداد مثل الحق والباطل الفجور والتقوى ، الحب والبغض ومئات من هذه النظائر التي لاتستطيع النظرية الجدلية الاجابة عليها .
6) The Criterion: What is the criterion for distinguishing error and truth in the dialectical school? Is it not scientific experiment? However, this criterion is completely sterile because experiment itself is not perceived by sense, but by primary rational axioms. There are principal concepts that cannot be perceived by abstract sense, such as cause and effect, necessity, and possibility, and no device can perceive them. And let us not forget to remind that animals possess the same sensory apparatus as humans. Likewise, this concept applies to the essence of distinguishing opposites, such as truth and falsehood, immorality and piety, love and hatred, and hundreds of such parallels that the dialectical theory cannot answer.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth criticism of dialectical materialism. The author questions the criterion (mi'yar) of truth used by the dialectical school: is it not scientific experiment? He responds that this criterion is sterile (aqim) because experiment itself is not grasped by the senses; rather, it depends on rational principles (al-usul al-'aqliyya al-badihiyya). Concepts like causality ('illa wa ma'lool), necessity (darura), and possibility (imkan) are not detectable by any sensory device. Moreover, animals share the same sensory organs as humans, yet they lack rational understanding. Therefore, the distinction between opposites (truth/falsehood, piety/immorality, love/hatred) cannot be made by sense alone. The author criticizes the theory's inability to account for such fundamental distinctions. For example, the ruling that wine is haram is derived from revelational and rational sources, not from experiment.

B) It depends on rational axioms that are not sensed

Key Takeaway
Scientific experiment cannot be the ultimate criterion of truth because it presupposes rational axioms that are themselves not empirically verifiable.

Critique of Economic Determinism and Hereditary Influence
Author challenges dialectical materialism to explain hereditary similarities and moral relativism of class.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's two-pronged critique of dialectical materialism regarding hereditary similarities and moral relativism.

Core Idea
Economic determinism cannot explain hereditary traits or universal ethics—two lethal blows to reflection theory.

This lesson examines a crucial critique of dialectical materialism. The author challenges the reflection theory by pointing out that it cannot account for the striking similarities in character and behavior among blood relatives, nor for the existence of universal moral values that transcend class boundaries. As we analyze this critique, you will learn how to deconstruct a philosophical argument and identify its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
First, identify the doctrine being criticized: dialectical materialism's reflection theory (nazariyyat al-'inksas), which claims that thought is merely a reflection of economic class struggle.
The target is explicitly named in the passage.
2
Extract the first critique: hereditary similarity
The author asks how reflection theory explains the 'amazing similarity between siblings' and across generations. This challenges the theory by introducing a non-economic factor (heredity) that shapes knowledge and character.
The author argues that heredity poses an insoluble problem for the theory.
3
Extract the second critique: moral relativism
The author then presents a reductio ad absurdum: if thought reflects class struggle, then the rich and poor would have opposite moral valuations—e.g., the rich might see lying as a virtue, the poor truthfulness. This would lead to moral relativism, which contradicts universal Islamic ethics.
The author implies that such relativism is absurd, given universal moral truths.
4
Assess the critique's method
The author uses two logical devices: (1) posing a question about an unexplained phenomenon (heredity), and (2) a reductio ad absurdum showing an implausible consequence (moral relativism). This is a common method in Islamic dialectical theology (jadal).
Notice how the critique does not rely on empirical data but on logical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷ ( التأثير الوراثي في المعرفة البشرية انها احدى المشكلات الكبرى لهذه النظرية فماذا تقول نظرية الانعكاس عن هذا التشابه العجيب بين الاخوة في تصرفاتهم وطباعهم وكذلك الامر بين الاجداد والابناء والاحفاد ؟ ( اذا كان العامل الاقتصادي هو المحرك للتاريخ الانساني وان الفكر ليس الا انعكاسا للصراع الاقتصادي الطبقي فسيكون للفقير لون من التفكير والاعتقاد يختلف عن لون التفكير والاعتقاد الذي يفكر به الغني فهل ترى النظرية الجدلية ان الغني مثلا يرى ان الكذب من مكارم الاخلاق في حين يعتقد الفقير ان الصدق من مكارم الاخلاق هل تتغير القيم الاخلاقية والوجدانية تبعا للمستوى المعيشي الذي يمر به الفرد ؟
7) Hereditary influence in human knowledge: It is one of the major problems of this theory. So what does the reflection theory say about this amazing similarity between siblings in their behaviors and dispositions, and likewise between grandparents, parents, and grandchildren? If the economic factor is the driver of human history and thought is nothing but a reflection of class economic struggle, then the poor person would have a type of thinking and belief different from the type of thinking and belief of the rich person. Does the dialectical theory hold that the rich, for example, see lying as a noble character while the poor believes that truthfulness is a noble character? Do ethical and emotional values change according to the living standard of the individual?
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh criticism. The author raises two interconnected problems for dialectical materialism. First, if knowledge is merely a reflection of material conditions, how can the reflection theory (nazariyyat al-'inksas) explain the striking similarity in character and behavior among blood relatives across generations? Heredity seems to play a role that cannot be reduced to economic factors. Second, if class struggle determines thought, then the poor and rich would have entirely different moral valuations. The author asks whether the dialectical theory would claim that the rich consider lying a virtue while the poor consider truthfulness a virtue, leading to moral relativism. This reductio ad absurdum highlights the implausibility of such a position. In Islamic ethics, truthfulness (sidq) is a universal virtue commanded for all believers regardless of class. For example, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Truthfulness leads to righteousness,' and this applies to rich and poor alike.

نظرية الانعكاس (nazariyyat al-'inksas): reflection theory
العامل الاقتصادي (al-'amil al-iqtisadi): economic factor

Key Takeaway
The author uses logical inconsistency (unexplained heredity) and reductio ad absurdum (moral relativism) to challenge dialectical materialism, showing that a sound epistemology must accommodate both hereditary factors and universal moral truths.

The Dual Nature of Man: A Refutation of Materialism
Author argues for dual material-spiritual nature and concludes critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two aspects of human nature presented by the author and explain how each challenges dialectical materialism.

Core Idea
Man is not mere matter; the interaction between body and spirit refutes dialectical materialism.

The author delivers his final critique against dialectical materialism by arguing that human beings are not purely material. He identifies a material aspect (the body) and a non-material spiritual aspect (the mind/soul), and demonstrates their bidirectional influence. This lesson will guide you through reconstructing his argument and understanding its force against materialist monism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects
Locate where the author states that humans have two sides: a material side (organic composition) and a spiritual non-material side (intellectual and mental activity). Note that the spiritual side is described as the 'arena' of thought, not a byproduct of matter.
The Arabic term for dual-natured is 'muzdawij al-shakhsiyya'.
2
Understand bidirectional influence
The author gives two examples: (1) seeing a ghost causes shivering (spirit affects body), and (2) old age weakens mental activity (body affects spirit). This shows that the two aspects constantly interact.
The interaction is mutual, not one-way.
3
Recognize the contradiction with materialism
Materialism claims that everything is matter and that consciousness is produced by matter. The author's examples show that the non-material spirit can cause physical reactions, which materialism cannot explain without reducing spirit to matter.
This is a direct critique of the monistic ontology of dialectical materialism.
4
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author states that the dialectical theory is a 'glittering scientific claim built on feeble foundations.' He admits he has only presented a few difficulties, not a comprehensive refutation.
The conclusion is that the theory cannot withstand organized thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸) ان للانسان جانبين احدهما مادي يتمثل في تركيبه العضوي والآخر روحي لامادي وهو مسرح النشاط الفكري والعقلي فليس الانسان مجرد مادة معقدة وانما هو مزدوج الشخصية من عنصر مادي وآخر لامادي وان العلاقة بينهما وثيقة حتى ان احدهما يؤثر في الآخر باستمرار فاذا رأى الشخص شبحا في الظلام اعترته القشعريرة فهذا من اثر الروح في الجسم واذا دبت الشيخوخة في الجسد وهن النشاط العقلي وهذا من اثر الجسد في الروح ! اننا في هذا الفصل المقتضب لانريد ان نتابع بشمولية ثغرات هذه النظرية الفاشلة في تفسير الادراك البشري و في تطور المعرفة لانه سيخرج الدراسة عن خطها العام . بل استعرضنا بعض الصعوبات التي واجهتها وثبت من خلالها ان النظرية الجدلية في المعرفة هي ادعاءات علمية ضخمة براقة قائمة على أسس واهية لاتصمد في مسرح التفكير المنظم.
8) Human beings have two aspects: one material, represented by their organic composition, and the other spiritual, non-material, which is the arena of intellectual and mental activity. So man is not just complex matter, but rather dual-natured, composed of a material element and a non-material element. The relationship between them is close, such that one constantly affects the other. When a person sees a ghost in darkness, he shudders; this is the effect of the spirit on the body. When old age creeps into the body, mental activity weakens; this is the effect of the body on the spirit. In this brief chapter we do not want to comprehensively follow the gaps of this failed theory in explaining human perception and development of knowledge, as that would take the study out of its general line. Rather, we have presented some of the difficulties it faced and through which it has become evident that the dialectical theory of knowledge is a huge, glittering scientific claim built on feeble foundations that cannot withstand the arena of organized thought.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth criticism and concludes the critique. The author asserts that humans have both a material body (jasad) and a non-material spirit (ruh), which interact closely. Two examples illustrate bidirectional influence: seeing a ghost (a spiritual experience) causes physical shivering (spirit affects body), and aging weakens the body leading to diminished mental activity (body affects spirit). This dualism directly challenges the materialistic monism of dialectical materialism. The author then states that he has only presented a few difficulties (points 5-8), not a comprehensive refutation. He concludes that the dialectical theory of knowledge is a grandiose but baseless claim that cannot withstand organized thought. In Islamic theology, the soul (ruh) is a non-material entity that survives the body, and its interaction with the body is affirmed in texts like the Qur'an: 'Allah takes the souls at the time of death' (Az-Zumar 42).

Key Terms
جانب مادي (janib maddi): Material aspect; the organic composition of the human body.
جانب روحي لامادي (janib ruhi la maddi): Spiritual non-material aspect; the seat of intellectual and mental activity.
مزدوج الشخصية (muzdawij al-shakhsiyya): Dual-natured; composed of both material and spiritual elements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Old age weakening intellect
B) Shivering when seeing a ghost
C) Learning from experiment
D) Economic struggle shaping thought

Key Takeaway
The bidirectional interaction between body and spirit demonstrates that man is not merely matter, providing a fatal flaw in dialectical materialism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Analyze the Qur'anic verse 'Allah takes the souls at the time of death' (Az-Zumar 42) in light of the author's dual-nature argument. How does this verse presuppose an immaterial soul that survives the body?

Three Gaps in the Stimulus-Response Theory of Knowledge
Author critiques the empirical stimulus-response epistemology and lists three gaps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain three logical gaps in the stimulus-response theory of knowledge: external description vs. internal mechanism, idea dependency on stimuli rather than evidence, and reduction of ideas to digestive processes.

Core Idea
Superficial observation, stimulus-dependency, and biological reductionism undermine any empirical theory of knowledge.

The empirical stimulus-response theory claims that knowledge arises from environmental triggers and behavioral responses. In this passage, the author systematically deconstructs this claim by exposing three fundamental gaps. As you follow the critique, you will learn to distinguish between superficial description and genuine understanding, recognize the difference between evidence and physical causes, and see why a purely empirical epistemology cannot adjudicate between competing worldviews.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the first gap: external description vs. internal knowledge
The author uses the camera example: pressing a button and seeing a picture does not mean one knows how the camera works. Identify the difference between observing an effect and understanding its cause. This gap shows that empirical observation alone cannot provide deep knowledge.
Consider how this relates to Islamic critiques of purely empirical knowledge.
2
Examine the second gap: ideas tied to stimuli, not evidence
The author argues that in this theory, ideas change with stimuli (e.g., food changes perception of truth). This leads to relativism and makes rational discussion impossible because the focus is on removing the material stimulus rather than evaluating evidence. Find the specific examples in the text.
Note the contrast with the Islamic concept of dalil (evidence) as the basis for rational judgment.
3
Explore the third gap: ideas as digestive processes
The author claims that the theory reduces the birth of ideas to digestion. Consequently, comparing different theories becomes like comparing digestive processes—absurd. This gap undermines any objective criterion for truth. The passage ends by listing competing theories (materialism vs. theology, Newton vs. Einstein, etc.) that cannot be adjudicated under this model.
Reflect on how this critique points to the need for a theory that respects rational evidence and allows for objective evaluation.

This theory is not free of gaps, and they can be summarized as follows: (1) Merely a superficial external description of stimulus and response is insufficient to explain knowledge, even if we assume the correctness of this theory. For if someone says that merely pressing the button of a camera makes it produce beautiful pictures because I saw that with my own eyes, even if we believe him and he is truthful in what he saw, that does not mean that he knows the essence of the parts of the camera, the nature of its movement, and how it was invented, through this superficial view of the camera. (2) The idea in this theory is linked to its stimuli, not to its evidence. It is possible for the idea to change to another contradictory idea if the stimulus and external condition change. It is futile to discuss a thinker's idea and its evidence; rather one must examine the material stimuli of that idea and remove them. For if a person eats roasted lamb, he will see truth as a pillar of good, but if he eats roasted fish, he must see truth as a pillar of evil, depending on the nature of the stimulus. And he may see 3+6=3 if he eats a tomato, and it becomes 3+3=8 if he eats a potato. (3) This theory of knowledge makes the birth of ideas similar to digestive processes, so it is impossible to investigate the correctness or error of any idea. We cannot ask which is correct: the materialism of Epicurus or the theology of Aristotle? Newton in his gravity or Einstein in his relativity? Marx in his dialectic or Ricardo in his continuity? Because such a question, in light of this theory, is entirely similar to the following question: which is correct, the digestive processes of
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a critical examination of the empirical stimulus-response theory of knowledge, often associated with behaviorist psychology. The author identifies three major gaps. First, a mere external, superficial description of a stimulus and response—like pressing a camera button and seeing a picture—does not constitute genuine knowledge of how the camera works internally. The example illustrates that observation without understanding the underlying mechanism fails to provide true knowledge. Second, the theory ties ideas to their physical stimuli rather than to rational evidence. The author gives a striking example: what one eats can determine what one considers true—roasted lamb leads to seeing truth as good, roasted fish as evil, and even arithmetic changes with food. This relativism makes rational discussion pointless because one must instead remove the material stimulus. Third, the theory reduces intellectual production to a biological process akin to digestion. Consequently, one cannot adjudicate between major philosophical or scientific theories—materialism vs. theology, Newton vs. Einstein, etc.—because such judgments would be like asking which digestive process is correct. The critique highlights the theory's inability to account for objective truth and rational evaluation.

Key Takeaway
The stimulus-response theory fails to account for genuine knowledge because it confuses external description with internal understanding, ties ideas to mutable physical stimuli, and reduces rational thought to digestive processes, making objective truth impossible.

Five Critiques of Materialist Epistemology
Author lists five objections to the stimulus-response theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five critiques the author levels against the stimulus-response theory and explain why they collectively refute materialism as a complete epistemology.

Core Idea
Five simple objections reveal why materialism cannot explain knowledge, truth, or moral judgment.

The author argues that the stimulus-response theory (behaviorism) is fundamentally inadequate to explain human knowledge and cognition. He lists five gaps in the theory, each targeting a different aspect: superficial observation, truth relativity, inability to evaluate truth, the mystery of perception, and the problem of moral contradiction. This lesson will walk you through each critique so you can see how the author builds a powerful cumulative case against reducing knowledge to mere physical reaction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the target theory
The stimulus-response theory claims all knowledge and behavior are conditioned reflexes to physical stimuli. Identify this claim in the passage's first line.
This is a modern materialist view, often associated with Pavlov.
2
First critique: Superficial observation
The author gives the example of pressing a camera button. Seeing that pressing yields a picture does not mean one understands the camera's internal mechanism. Point out why this shows that stimulus-response fails to capture knowledge of essences.
Compare to Islamic epistemology's distinction between 'ilm al-haqiqa (knowledge of reality) and mere sense impression.
3
Second critique: Truth relativism
If ideas are tied to stimuli, then changing the stimulus can change the idea, even to a contradiction. The author gives absurd examples (3+6=3 after eating a tomato). Explain how this undercuts any notion of stable truth.
This objection shows the theory leads to radical relativism, incompatible with divine revelation.
4
Third critique: No truth evaluation
The author compares ideas to digestive processes—both are mere bodily functions. If so, asking whether an idea is true or false is as meaningless as asking whether digestion is true. Summarize why this eliminates the possibility of assessing any claim, including the theory itself.
This is a self-referential problem: if the theory is true, it cannot claim truth.
5
Fourth critique: Emergence of perception
If perception is purely a physiological interaction between brain cells and external objects, where does the conscious experience of 'perception' come from? The author argues it is something qualitatively different—a 'something else' not reducible to material components.
This resonates with the Islamic concept of ruh (soul) as the locus of cognition.

This theory is not free of gaps, which can be summarized as follows: (1) Mere superficial external description of stimulus and response is insufficient to explain knowledge. Even if we assume this theory is true, if someone says that merely pressing the camera button makes it produce beautiful pictures because he saw that with his own eyes, even if we believe him and he is truthful in what he saw, that does not mean he knows the essence of the camera's parts, the nature of its movement, and its invention mechanism through this superficial look at the camera. (2) In this theory, an idea is linked to its stimuli, not to its evidence. It is possible for an idea to change to a contradictory idea if the stimulus and external condition differ. It is futile to debate a thinker about his idea and its evidence; rather, one must investigate the material stimuli of that idea and remove them. If a person eats a roasted lamb, he will see truth as a pillar of good; but if he eats a roasted fish, he will inevitably see truth as a pillar of evil, depending on the nature of the stimulus. And he may see 3+6=3 if he eats a tomato, and it becomes 3+3=8 if he eats a potato. (3) This theory of knowledge makes the birth of ideas similar to digestive processes. One cannot investigate the truth or falsehood of any idea. We cannot ask who is correct: the materialism of Epicurus or the theism of Aristotle? Newton in his gravity or Einstein in his relativity? Marx in his dialectic or Ricardo in his laissez-faire? Because such a question, in light of this theory, is exactly like asking: which is correct, the digestive processes of Epicurus, Newton, and Marx, or those of Aristotle, Einstein, and Ricardo? The ideas of these thinkers are like the digestive processes in their stomachs—nothing but bodily functions and organic activities. And since it is not permissible to ask whether the stomach's activity is true or false, so too with intellectual activity. (4) If perception is a purely physiological process and a special material interaction between brain cells and material sensory things, then the manner of functioning of these brain cells must be linked to the nature of sensation without addition. So where does perception come from? It is something else different from these components or stimuli or excitations and responses. (5) This stimulus-response theory cannot answer the question: How does a person distinguish between opposites: truth and falsehood, good and evil, beauty and ugliness, and many others? Because this theory considers man as a mechanical machine that has no feeling. And what does it say about the emergence of contradictions from one man in one situation? Let us take an example from reality: among the strange phenomena that have perplexed all psychologists is the emergence of contradictions from one person in one issue. A handsome teenage youth attempts to have sex without your knowledge with your sister or daughter, which provokes in you a stormy reaction that shakes your entire being, conscience, body, and feelings, and the emotion may be deadly hysteria. When that same youth comes as a suitor to marry that girl, the same man receives him with a calm mind and open heart, even though the subject is the same. What does Pavlov say?
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a critical assessment of the materialist/epiricist theory of knowledge, specifically the stimulus-response (Pavlovian) model. The author enumerates five gaps in the theory, each presented as an independent objection. The first objection argues that mere observation of external input-output correlation does not yield understanding of the essence or mechanism of things. The author uses the analogy of pressing a camera button: seeing that pressing yields pictures does not mean one knows the camera's internal structure. This parallels Islamic epistemology's insistence on 'ilm al-haqiqa (knowledge of reality) beyond mere sense impression. The second objection targets the theory's relativistic consequence: since ideas are tied to material stimuli, they can change arbitrarily with external conditions, making truth contingent on diet or environment. The author gives absurd examples (e.g., 3+6=3 after eating a tomato) to illustrate that such a theory cannot account for logical necessity. The third objection extends this: comparing ideas to digestion eliminates the possibility of evaluating truth. If ideas are mere bodily secretions, asking about their correctness is as meaningless as asking if digestion is true. The author lists prominent thinkers to show that on this view, all intellectual systems are equally valueless physiological events. The fourth objection: if perception is purely physiological, it cannot explain the emergence of 'perception' (idrak) as a distinct cognitive reality over and above neural processes. There is a qualitative leap from material interaction to conscious awareness—something the theory cannot attribute. The fifth objection concerns the ability to distinguish opposites (truth/falsehood, good/evil, etc.) and handle contradictions. The author gives a vivid example: a man reacts with outrage when a youth attempts illicit sex with his daughter, but welcomes the same youth as a suitor. The same act viewed differently—this shows human judgment is not mechanically determined by stimuli. The author concludes by challenging Pavlov to explain this phenomenon. Throughout, the argument aligns with Islamic critiques of empiricism, emphasizing the transcendental nature of human knowledge and moral discernment.

Key Terms

منبهات

Critique of Pavlov's Reflex Theory of Knowledge
Author critiques Pavlov's stimulus-response theory as unable to explain human judgment and contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological weaknesses of Pavlov's stimulus-response theory as presented in the passage and relate them to Islamic critiques of materialism.

Core Idea
Knowledge involves qualitative judgments that no mechanical reflex theory can explain.

This passage presents a powerful critique of Pavlov's reflex theory by showing that reducing human knowledge to physical stimulus-response mechanisms cannot account for fundamental aspects of our cognition. The author argues that perception, moral distinction, contradiction, and memory all transcend mere physiology. Understanding this critique sharpens our ability to recognize the limits of materialist accounts of knowledge, a key concern in Islamic theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's opening analogy
The author compares the ideas of famous thinkers to digestion—both are merely physical processes, so they cannot be judged true or false. This sets up the reductionist view of knowledge that will be criticized.
Notice the implicit rejection of reducing intellectual activity to bodily functions.
2
Extract the claim about perception
The author states that if perception is purely physiological, it must be directly linked to sensation without addition. But then asks: where does perception come from? This challenges the sufficiency of material interactions to produce consciousness.
The argument is that perception is something more than its physical components.
3
Recognize the problem of distinguishing opposites
The theory cannot explain how humans distinguish between fundamental opposites like truth/falsehood, good/evil, beauty/ugliness. This is because it treats humans as unconscious machines.
This is a qualitative, not quantitative, distinction that requires consciousness.
4
Analyze the real-world contradiction example
The author presents a case where the same person reacts violently to a sexual advance toward a relative but welcomes the same person as a suitor for marriage. This contradiction shows that human judgment involves more than learned reflexes—it involves moral and social frameworks.
The example highlights how context and intention affect judgment, which materialist models cannot capture.
5
Understand the memory challenge
The theory cannot explain human memory, which stores billions of images, concepts, and memories. This is a major gap because memory is a crucial tool of knowledge.
Memory involves storage and retrieval that go beyond simple stimulus-response.

أبيقور ونيوتن وماركس او عند ارسطو و اينشتاين و ريكاردو • فافكار هؤلاء المفكرين كعمليات الهضم في معدهم ليست الا وظائف جسمية ونشاطات عضوية ومادام لا يجوز ان نتساءل عن نشاط المعدة هل هو صادق او كاذب فكذلك بالنسبة الى النشاط الفكري. اذا كان الادراك عملية فسلجية خالصة وتفاعل مادي خاص بين الخلايا المخية والاشياء المادية الحسية فيجب ان تكون كيفية عمل هذه الخلايا المخية مرتبط بطبيعة الاحساس من دون زيادة فمن أين جاء الادراك؟ وهو شيء آخر مختلف عن هذه المكونات أو المنبهات أو الاثارات والاستجابات. لاتستطيع نظرية الاثارة هذه ان تجيب على التساؤل الذي يقول: كيف يميز الانسان بين الاضداد. الحق والباطل. الخير والشر. الجمال والقبح وغيرها الكثير لانها أي هذه النظرية عدت الانسان بوصفه آلة ميكانيكية لا يوجد لديها شعور. وماذا يقول عن صدور المتناقضات من رجل واحد في موقف واحد؟ ولنأخذ مثال من ارض الواقع: ويعد من الظواهر العجيبة التي حيرت علماء النفس مجتمعين وهو صدور المتناقضات عن الشخص الواحد في مسألة واحدة: يتقدم شاب وسيم مراهق بمحاولة ممارسة الجنس من دون علمك مع شقيقتك او ابنتك فيثير ذلك عندك رد فعل عاصف يهتز له كل كيانك وضميرك وجسدك ووجدانك وقد يكون الانفعال هستيريا قاتلا، وعندما يأتي ذلك الشاب خاطبا تلك الفتاة لغرض الزواج يتلقاه ذلك الرجل نفسه بعقل هادىء وصدر رحب مع ان الموضوع واحد. ماذا يجيب بافلوف؟ لاتستطيع هذه النظرية تفسير الذاكرة البشرية التي تتكون من مليارات الصور والمفاهيم والذكريات. فلا يوجد أي مساحة لتأويل الذاكرة التي هي واحدة من أهم ادوات المعرفة. والخلاصة: ان تجارب بافلوف المختبرية والميدانية والنفسية لاكتشاف الاثر الفسلجي للوعي البشري على خلايا المخ تحقق فوائد علمية وطبية لاغبار عليها ولكن تفسير المعرفة بهذه الطريقة السطحية الساذجة امر لا يقبله كل ذي عقل لبيب.
Epicurus, Newton, Marx, or Aristotle, Einstein, and Ricardo: the ideas of these thinkers are like the processes of digestion in their stomach—nothing but bodily functions and organic activities. Since it is not permissible to ask whether the activity of the stomach is true or false, likewise for intellectual activity. If perception is a pure physiological process and a specific material interaction between brain cells and material sensory objects, then the mode of operation of these brain cells must be linked to the nature of sensation without addition. Where then does perception come from? It is something else different from these components, stimuli, excitations, and responses. This stimulus-response theory cannot answer the question: how does a human distinguish between opposites—truth and falsehood, good and evil, beauty and ugliness, and many others—because this theory considers the human as a mechanical machine that has no feeling. And what does it say about the emergence of contradictions from one man in one situation? Let us take a real-world example: It is considered one of the amazing phenomena that have baffled psychologists collectively, namely the emergence of contradictions from a single person in one matter: A handsome teenage boy attempts to engage in sexual relations without your knowledge with your sister or daughter, and this provokes in you a stormy reaction that shakes your entire being, conscience, body, and feelings; it may be a murderous hysterical outburst. When that same boy comes asking for that girl's hand in marriage, the same man receives him with a calm mind and open heart, even though the subject is the same. What does Pavlov answer? This theory cannot explain human memory, which consists of billions of images, concepts, and memories. There is no room to interpret memory, which is one of the most important tools of knowledge. The conclusion: Pavlov's laboratory, field, and psychological experiments to discover the physiological effect of human consciousness on brain cells achieve unquestionable scientific and medical benefits, but explaining knowledge in this superficial and naive way is unacceptable to every intelligent person.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage critiques Pavlov's reflex theory as an epistemological theory. It opens by comparing materialist thinkers' ideas to digestion—both are merely physical processes, so they cannot be judged true or false. This reduces intellectual knowledge to bodily functions. The author then argues that if perception is purely physiological, it cannot account for the origin of perception itself, which transcends physical stimuli. The stimulus-response theory is shown to be unable to explain how humans distinguish between opposites such as truth/falsehood, good/evil, etc., because it treats humans as unconscious machines. A powerful real-world example is given: the same person reacts violently to a sexual advance toward his sister/daughter but warmly welcomes the same boy as a suitor for marriage. This contradiction highlights that human judgment involves more than conditioned reflexes. The passage concludes that while Pavlov's experiments have scientific value, his reductive theory of knowledge is intellectually unacceptable. In the context of Islamic thought, this critique aligns with the rejection of materialist accounts of the soul (nafs) and the affirmation that knowledge involves a non-physical faculty that distinguishes truth and falsehood.

Stream of Consciousness and the Need for Islamic Epistemology
Author presents James's stream of consciousness theory and raises questions it cannot answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the limitations of a pure stream-of-consciousness epistemology and articulate the need for the concept of substance (jawhar) in Islamic theory of knowledge.

Core Idea
A purely continuous stream cannot explain how knowledge forms distinct concepts—Islamic epistemology supplies the missing link.

William James's stream of consciousness theory presents knowledge as a continuous flow, yet he admits experience condenses into blocks. This raises fundamental questions that his theory cannot answer. In this lesson, we will use tools from Islamic epistemology to diagnose why this model fails and what is needed to account for the formation of universal concepts from particular experiences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim of James's theory
The passage states that experience gives a flowing stream, not discrete atoms. Pinpoint the analogy of a river versus mosaic tiles.
The passage says: 'تيارا من الشعور سيالا مثل النهر الجاري'
2
Locate where James admits experience condenses into substances
Find the sentence: 'صحيح ان التجربة تميل الى ان تتكدس وتغلظ على هيئة جواهر'. This is a crucial concession.
The term 'جواهر' is key—it hints at the Islamic concept of substances.
3
Extract the two unanswered questions posed at the end
The passage asks why experience unites into blocks and why some blocks are denser. These questions reveal a gap in the theory.
Questions: 'لماذا تتحد التجربة في كتل؟ ولماذا تكون أسمك وأصلب هنا مما هي هنالك؟'
4
Link to Islamic epistemology: How does the concept of jawhar and the theory of abstraction (tajrid) address these questions?
Islamic philosophers like Ibn Sina argue that universals are abstracted from particulars through the intellect, which provides the 'substance' of concepts that James's theory cannot explain.
This shows the necessity of the intellect as an active agent in knowledge formation.

نظرية السيل الشعوري: رائدها ومهندسها الفيلسوف وعالم النفس الامريكي وليم جيمس (1842-1915م). تقول هذه النظرية ان المعرفة قائمة على اساس التجربة التي تعطينا تيارا من الشعور سيالا مثل النهر الجاري وليس تيارا من الذرات المنفصلة التي رص بعضها مع البعض كقطع الموزاييك. ان تيار الشعور تتداخل فيه الاشياء بعضها في بعض زمانا ومكانا ويتداخل فيها السابق واللاحق وكل شيء فيه يسيل ويتدفق. صحيح ان التجربة تميل الى ان تتكدس وتغلظ على هيئة جواهر ولكن هذه المواد تميل الى ان تنصهر في بعضها البعض لتصبح مادة سائلة تغلظ بدورها على شكل مواد جديدة. وامام هذه النظرية اسئلة لاتستطيع الاجابة عنها وهي: لماذا تتحد التجربة في كتل؟ ولماذا تكون أسمك وأصلب هنا مما هي هنالك؟ وكيف
The Stream of Consciousness Theory: Its pioneer and engineer is the American philosopher and psychologist William James (1842-1915). This theory states that knowledge is based on experience which gives us a flowing stream of consciousness like a flowing river, not a stream of discrete atoms pieced together like mosaic tiles. In the stream of consciousness, things interpenetrate each other in time and space, the prior and subsequent interpenetrate, and everything flows and streams. It is true that experience tends to condense and thicken into substances, but these substances tend to melt into each other to become a liquid substance that in turn thickens into new substances. Before this theory are questions it cannot answer: Why does experience unite into blocks? Why is it denser and harder here than there? And how
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents William James's stream of consciousness theory and then raises critical questions about it. James proposes that knowledge comes from experience as a continuous, flowing stream, like a river, rather than as a sequence of discrete atomic units (like mosaic pieces). He emphasizes that consciousness is fluid, with past and present interpenetrating. However, James acknowledges that experience tends to coalesce into distinct 'substances' or blocks, but these blocks are not permanent—they can melt and reform. The passage then lists questions that the theory cannot answer: why does experience form unified blocks? Why are some blocks denser or harder than others? And how does this process occur? This critique highlights that a purely continuous model struggles to explain the formation of distinct objects and concepts in consciousness. In Islamic thought, similar issues arise in discussions of how universal concepts (kulliyat) are abstracted from particular experiences.

James's stream of consciousness theory fails to explain how continuous experience gives rise to discrete conceptual blocks; Islamic epistemology provides the missing mechanism through abstraction of universals.

Critique of Reducing Knowledge to Brain Physiology
Critique of Pavlov's reductionist theory of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key objections to a physiological reductionist account of knowledge and evaluate its shortcomings from an Islamic epistemological perspective.

Core Idea
Reducing knowledge to brain processes fails to account for the depth of memory and meaning.

This passage critiques Pavlov's attempt to explain all knowledge through physiological experiments on the brain. The author acknowledges the medical benefits but rejects the superficial interpretation of knowledge. In Islamic thought, knowledge ('ilm) is linked to the soul and divine preservation, not mere biology. We will dissect this critique and see how it aligns with the Islamic epistemological framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Find the theory being attacked. The passage explicitly names Pavlov's theory of explaining knowledge through brain physiology.
2
Extract the main objection
The author says the theory cannot explain memory, which is a key tool of knowledge. Memory consists of billions of images and concepts.
3
Note the concession
Observe that the author does not reject Pavlov's experiments wholesale; they grant their medical and scientific value.
4
Assess the epistemological alternative implied
The critique suggests that knowledge, especially memory, requires a non-physiological explanation, aligning with Islamic views on the soul and divine preservation.
This step connects the critique to Islamic epistemology.

Memory is a crucial tool for knowledge, yet Pavlov's physiological approach cannot explain it. The author acknowledges the scientific value of Pavlov's experiments for medicine and biology, but strongly rejects the reduction of all knowledge to mere physiological processes. The objection is that such a view is too simplistic and ignores the richness of human cognition. In Islamic epistemology, memory is also tied to the soul and spiritual preservation (hifz), as seen in the memorization of the Qur'an both in hearts and books, transcending mere brain activity.

Critique of Stream of Consciousness: Memory and Imagination in Islamic Epistemology
Author critiques James' stream of consciousness theory using memory and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in James' stream of consciousness theory by contrasting it with the Islamic epistemological role of memory and imagination in transcending immediate experience.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not merely a stream of present consciousness; it transcends time through memory and imagination.

William James proposed that knowledge is a continuous flow of immediate experience. However, this view fails to account for how we know past and future events. In this lesson, we examine the logical gaps in his theory and see how Islamic epistemology properly accounts for the role of memory and imagination in acquiring knowledge beyond the present moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim of James' theory
Read the opening lines: 'This theory says that knowledge is based on experience which gives us a stream of consciousness flowing like a running river.' Note that knowledge is reduced to a continuous present experience.
2
List the unanswerable questions
The author poses six questions that the theory cannot answer, such as 'Why does experience unite into blocks?' and 'Why do we say some ideas are true and others false?' These point to the theory's explanatory limitations.
3
Examine James' attempted answer
James responds that consciousness actively selects and shapes reality based on interest and attention. This still confines knowledge to the immediate present.
4
Present the author's critique
The author argues that the content of consciousness includes ideas from memory and imagination that draw on past sensory perceptions, providing knowledge of past and future experiences, which transcends present sense data.
This is the key logical flaw: the theory cannot account for knowledge that is not directly present.
5
Connect to Islamic epistemology
In Islamic thought, memory (dhakira) and imagination (mukhayyala) are faculties that enable knowledge of the unseen (ghayb) and historical events. For example, the transmission of hadith relies on memory, and analogical reasoning (qiyas) applies past rulings to new cases.
This shows that a complete epistemological theory must include these faculties.

تقول هذه النظرية ان المعرفة قائمة على اساس التجربة التي تعطينا تيارا من الشعور سيالا مثل النهر الجاري وليس تيارا من الذرات المنفصلة التي رص بعضها مع البعض كقطع الموزاييك . ان تيار الشعور تتداخل فيه الاشياء بعضها في بعض زمانا ومكانا ويتداخل فيها السابق واللاحق وكل شيء فيه يسيل ويتدفق . صحيح ان التجربة تميل الى ان تتكدس وتغلظ على هيئة جواهر ولكن هذه المواد تميل الى ان تنصهر في بعضها البعض لتصبح مادة سائلة تغلظ بدورها على شكل مواد جديدة . وامام هذه النظرية اسئلة لاتستطيع الاجابة عنها وهي : لماذا تتحد التجربة في كتل ؟ ولماذا تكون اسمك واصلب ههنا مما هي هنالك ؟ وكيف تؤلف عالما ؟ ولماذا تكون هذا العالم بالذات من دون غيره ؟ وكيف توجد افكار تعبر عن نفسها ؟ ولماذا نقول عن بعض هذه الافكار انها صحيحة ونقول عن بعضها الآخر انها باطلة ؟ ويجيب وليم جيمس : ان عالم الاشياء هو من صنعنا نحن البشر ذلك ان الشعور يتجلى عن اهتمام وانتباه . انه ارادي وحسي معا فهو ليس فقط يحس بل هو يؤثر بعضه في بعضه الآخر ويكره ويحب ويتلبث عند بعض مضمونه ويرفض الآخر أو ينساه ويسقطه فما ينتقيه ويؤثره ويختاره يعده حقيقيا وحيويا وما يرفضه وينبذه يعده غير مهم نسبيا وغير حقيقي . ان تبرير السيد وليم جيمس هذا فاشل تماما لان مضمون الشعور لا يتألف من معطيات الحس الحاضرة مباشرة بل يوجد الى جانب التجربة التي نعانيها الآن في الحاضر افكار تمكننا الذاكرة والمخيلة من استخراجها من الادراكات الحسية. وهذه الافكار تنهل من امور تتجاوز التجربة الحسية الحاضرة وتزودنا بمعرفة عن تجربة ماضية واخرى مستقبلية. فالفكرة المدركة لا تنحصر في الفعل الحاضر الآني بل لها علاقات مع مايسبقها وما يتلوها. يتبين من هذه المداخلة ان نظرية السيل الشعوري لاتستطيع الصمود مع وجود هذه المنعطفات الصعبة والاسئلة المحيرة .
This theory says that knowledge is based on experience which gives us a stream of consciousness flowing like a running river, not a stream of separate atoms arranged together like mosaic pieces. In the stream of consciousness, things interpenetrate each other in time and place, and the preceding and following interpenetrate, and everything flows and streams. It is true that experience tends to compact and thicken into substances, but these substances tend to melt into each other to become a liquid that in turn thickens into new substances. Before this theory are questions it cannot answer: Why does experience unite into blocks? Why is it thicker and harder here than there? How does it compose a world? Why is this particular world without another? How do ideas that express themselves come to be? Why do we say some of these ideas are true and others false? William James answers: The world of things is our own making, because consciousness manifests attention and interest. It is both voluntary and sensory; it not only senses but also influences itself, hates, loves, lingers on some of its content, rejects or forgets or drops the other. What it selects, prefers, and chooses it considers real and vital; what it rejects and pushes aside it considers relatively unimportant and unreal. This justification of Mr. William James is completely unsuccessful because the content of consciousness does not consist solely of immediate sense data; rather, alongside the experience we are currently undergoing, there are ideas that memory and imagination enable us to extract from sensory perceptions. These ideas draw from matters that transcend the present sensory experience and provide us with knowledge of past and future experience. The perceived idea is not limited to the present momentary act but has relationships with what precedes and follows it. From this intervention it becomes clear that the stream of consciousness theory cannot withstand these difficult turns and perplexing questions.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage first explains William James' theory of the stream of consciousness, then raises several objections and presents the author's own critique. The theory asserts that knowledge is a continuous flow of conscious experience, not a collection of discrete elements. James argues that experience coalesces into substances, but these are not fixed; they continuously merge. The author then poses six questions that the theory cannot answer, such as why experience forms into blocks and why some ideas are considered true and others false. James' response is that consciousness actively selects and shapes reality based on interest and attention. The author refutes this by pointing out that consciousness includes memory and imagination, which draw on past experiences and go beyond the present sensory input. Thus, knowledge is not just an immediate stream; it involves transcendence over time. This critique aligns with Islamic epistemology, which recognizes that knowledge (ilm) includes sensory, intellectual, and transmitted (naqli) elements, and that the soul (nafs) retains knowledge beyond the present moment through memory (hifz) and reason (aql).

Critique of James' Stream of Consciousness
Author poses epistemological questions, presents James' response, and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological flaw in William James' theory of consciousness as presented in the critique, and explain why the appeal to memory and imagination undermines his position.

Core Idea
James' stream of consciousness fails because it ignores memory and imagination that extend beyond immediate sensation.

In this passage, the author engages with William James' pragmatic view that consciousness constructs reality through selective attention. He poses critical questions about the nature of ideas, truth, and the world, then presents James' answer and refutes it. By dissecting this refutation, you will learn how an epistemological critique can expose the limits of a purely sensationalist account of consciousness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target theory
Find William James' claim about consciousness in the passage. He asserts that the world of things is our own making, as consciousness manifests as interest and attention, selecting some sensory content as real and vital.
This is a pragmatic view that reduces reality to what is attended to.
2
Extract the author's objections
List the specific points the author makes against James. The author argues that the content of consciousness is not limited to immediate sensory data; it includes ideas from memory and imagination that transcend present experience.
The objection is empirical: James ignores the role of memory and imagination.
3
Analyze the role of memory and imagination
Why does the author consider these crucial? Because they provide knowledge of past and future experiences, showing that the perceived idea has relations beyond the present act.
This challenges James' focus on the immediate stream.
4
Evaluate the critique's strength
Assess whether the author successfully undermines the theory. The critique exposes a gap in James' account: it cannot explain how ideas that rely on memory and imagination arise from mere selective attention.
The author concludes the theory 'cannot withstand' these objections.
