تؤلف عالما ؟ ولماذا تكون هذا العالم بالذات من دون غيره ؟ وكيف توجد افكار تعبر عن نفسها ؟ ولماذا نقول عن بعض هذه الافكار انها صحيحة ونقول عن بعضها الآخر انها باطلة ؟ ويجيب وليم جيمس : ان عالم الاشياء هو من صنعنا نحن البشر ذلك ان الشعور يتجلى عن اهتمام وانتباه . انه ارادي وحسي معا فهو ليس فقط يحس بل هو يؤثر بعضه في بعضه الآخر ويكره ويحب ويتلبث عند بعض مضمونه ويرفض الآخر أو ينساه ويسقطه فما ينتقيه ويؤثره ويختاره يعده حقيقيا وحيويا وما يرفضه وينبذه يعده غير مهم نسبيا وغير حقيقي ان تبرير السيد وليم جيمس هذا فاشل تماما لان مضمون الشعور لا يتألف من معطيات الحس الحاضرة مباشرة بل يوجد الى جانب التجربة التي نعانيها الآن في الحاضر افكار تمكننا الذاكرة والمخيلة من استخراجها من الادراكات الحسية. وهذه الافكار تنهل من امور تتجاوز التجربة الحسية الحاضرة وتزودنا بمعرفة عن تجربة ماضية واخرى مستقبلية. فالفكرة المدركة لا تنحصر في الفعل الحاضر الآني بل لها علاقات مع مايسبقها وما يتلوها) يتبين من هذه المداخلة ان نظرية السيل الشعوري لاتستطيع الصمود مع وجود هذه المنعطفات الصعبة والاسئلة المحيرة .
Do you create a world? And why is this world specifically without another? And how do ideas that express themselves exist? And why do we say about some of these ideas that they are true and about others that they are false? And William James answers: The world of things is of our own making, because consciousness manifests as interest and attention. It is both voluntary and sensory; it not only senses but influences some parts of it upon others, hates and loves, lingers on some of its content, and rejects or forgets or drops others. So what it selects, prefers, and chooses, it considers real and vital, and what it rejects and spurns, it considers relatively unimportant and unreal. This justification of Mr. William James is completely unsuccessful because the content of consciousness does not consist of immediate sensory data but rather, alongside the experience we now experience in the present, there are ideas that memory and imagination enable us to extract from sensory perceptions. And these ideas draw from matters that transcend present sensory experience and provide us with knowledge about past and future experience. So the perceived idea is not limited to the present immediate act but has relations with what precedes and follows it. It becomes clear from this intervention that the theory of the stream of consciousness cannot withstand these difficult turns and perplexing questions.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of William James's pragmatic/empiricist theory of consciousness, specifically his view that reality is constructed by human consciousness through selective attention. The author begins by posing rhetorical questions about the nature of the world, the existence of ideas, and the criteria of truth and falsehood. These questions set up James's answer: the world of things is of our own making, as consciousness manifests as interest and attention, being both volitional and sensory. According to James, consciousness selects, prefers, and chooses some content as real and vital, while rejecting other content as relatively unimportant and unreal. The author then refutes James's justification. The refutation is based on the fact that the content of consciousness is not limited to immediate sensory data. Rather, alongside present experience, there are ideas that memory and imagination extract from past sensory perceptions. These ideas provide knowledge about past and future experiences, transcending the present sensory moment. Therefore, the perceived idea has relations with what precedes and follows it, implying a temporal continuity that James's stream-of-consciousness theory cannot adequately explain. The author concludes that the theory fails because it cannot withstand these objections. The passage exemplifies an epistemological argument about the nature of knowledge and consciousness, grounding the critique in the role of memory and imagination.

Key Terms
الشعور (al-shu'ur): Consciousness, defined by James as a stream of selective attention that constructs reality; the author critiques this definition by arguing that it includes memory and imagination beyond immediate sensation. [Note: No significant shift — term is stable in philosophical discourse.]
السيل الشعوري (al-sayl al-shu'uri): Stream of consciousness; the theory attributed to William James that consciousness is a continuous flow of thoughts and sensations. [Note: Term is borrowed from Western philosophy; not a standard Islamic philosophical term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason William James's theory of consciousness fails?
A) It ignores the role of attention and interest.
B) It assumes consciousness is purely passive.
C) It cannot account for memory and imagination that transcend immediate sensation.
D) It relies too heavily on sensory data.

Key Takeaway
The critique of James' theory shows that consciousness is not merely selective attention to immediate sensation, but involves memory and imagination that connect present experience to past and future, thereby transcending the present moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1) Identify the main claim of William James as presented in the passage. Then, write a brief refutation using the author's arguments about memory and imagination. Make sure to contrast immediate sensation with ideas extracted from memory.
?
2) Consider the Islamic epistemological distinction between 'aql (reason) and hiss (sense). How does the author's critique align with the Islamic view that knowledge transcends sensory experience? Write a short paragraph.

Critique of the Three-Stage Theory and the Necessity of Rational Principles
Author presents and criticizes a positivist three-stage theory of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three criticisms of the three-stage theory of knowledge and explain why rational principles are indispensable for scientific knowledge.

Core Idea
Experiment without reason is blind; the three stages collapse without primary rational principles.

In this passage, the author presents a theory that knowledge progresses from sensory experience to scientific laws to philosophical synthesis. He then levels three powerful criticisms that expose the theory's reliance on unacknowledged rational foundations. As we walk through these criticisms, pay attention to how the author defends the primacy of 'aql over pure empiricism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three stages of knowledge
Read the description of the theory: sensory stage (observation of a single event), scientific stage (repeated observation leads to a general law), philosophical stage (synthesis of laws from multiple sciences). Note the claim that each stage is a premise for the next.
The theory is presented neutrally before criticism.
2
Understand the first criticism: experiment needs reason
The author argues that experiment alone, even if repeated millions of times, cannot produce a scientific law without the 'intellectual criterion' (al-mizan al-‘aqli). This is because a universal law requires an inference that goes beyond particulars, which only reason can provide.
This mirrors the Islamic epistemological principle that sense perception is insufficient for certain knowledge.
3
Understand the second criticism: scientific and philosophical knowledge are distinct
Philosophical knowledge deals with general topics not restricted by law and is independent of material experiment, while scientific knowledge is based on properties of materials. Therefore scientific knowledge does not automatically lead to philosophical knowledge.
This refutes the theory's assumption of a necessary progression.
4
Understand the third criticism: all stages depend on primary rational principles
The author asserts that the three stages have no cognitive value without primary rational principles (al-mabadi' al-‘aqliyya al-awwaliyya). These principles are foundational and not derived from experience; they underpin all thinking and deduction.
This is a direct refutation of empiricist epistemology.
5
Synthesize the critique
Consider how the three criticisms collectively show that the theory fails to acknowledge the a priori rational foundations of knowledge. The stages are not autonomous but depend on reason at every step.
This critique aligns with the Islamic emphasis on the role of 'aql in acquiring certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفاد هذه النظرية ( ان كل معرفة متقدمة تكون مقدمة لحصول معرفة أوسع وأكمل . فاذا لاحظ مريض ما مبتلى بالصداع مثلا انه قد بريء عند استعمال عشبة طبية معينة فهذه هي المرحلة الحسية فاذا تكرر العلاج مرات عدة واثبت انه مبرأ من الصداع فيستنتج قانون أو قاعدة علمية عامة وهذه هي المرحلة العلمية. أما المرحلة الفلسفية فهي عبارة عن المعرفة العامة المنتزعة من غالب العلوم وهي معرفة فوق المعرفة العلمية تجمع القوانين العلمية في تصور عام كما هي قوانين العلوم الفيزيائية والكيميائية والعلاقات المسيطرة عليها ويؤاخذ على هذه النظرية :
(۱) ان هذه المراحل للمعرفة لا يمكن انكارها ولكن المعرفة العلمية تتم عن طريق التجربة التي تحتاج بدورها الى الميزان العقلي لان التجربة بذاتها لا تعطي قانونا في مجال العلم ولو تكررت ملايين المرات . فلولا الميزان العقلي لما امكن للتجربة الوصول الى المرحلة العلمية. (۲) ان المعرفة الفلسفية تختلف عن المعرفة العلمية لان الاولى تبحث في مواضيع عامة غير مقيدة بقانون ولا علاقة لها بالتجربة المادية في حين ان المعرفة العلمية قائمة على اساس خواص المواد كماهو ملاحظ في علم الفيزياء وعلم الكيمياء مثلا ومن ثم لا يمكن ان تؤدي المعرفة العلمية حتما الى المعرفة الفلسفية.
۳) ان المراحل الحسية والعلمية والفلسفية لا يمكن لها ان تكون ذات قيمة معرفية لولا وجود المباديء العقلية الاولية . فهي لاتاتي بعفوية مفروضة من المجهول وانما تعتمد في اصل تكونها على اسس راسخة للتفكير والاستنباط .
The import of this theory (is that every advanced knowledge is a premise for obtaining a wider and more complete knowledge. So if a patient suffering from headache, for example, observes that he was cured when using a certain medicinal herb, this is the sensory stage. Then if the treatment is repeated several times and proves to be a cure for headache, a general scientific law or rule is inferred, and this is the scientific stage. As for the philosophical stage, it is the general knowledge derived from most sciences, and it is knowledge above scientific knowledge that gathers scientific laws in a general conception, such as the laws of physical and chemical sciences and the relations controlling them. And this theory is criticized:
(1) These stages of knowledge cannot be denied, but scientific knowledge occurs through experiment which in turn needs the intellectual criterion (al-mizan al-‘aqli) because experiment by itself does not give a law in the field of science, even if repeated millions of times. For without the intellectual criterion, it would not be possible for experiment to reach the scientific stage.
(2) Philosophical knowledge differs from scientific knowledge because the former investigates general topics not restricted by a law and has no relation to material experiment, while scientific knowledge is based on the properties of materials as observed in physics and chemistry, for example. Hence, scientific knowledge cannot necessarily lead to philosophical knowledge.
(3) The sensory, scientific, and philosophical stages cannot be of any cognitive value without the existence of primary rational principles. They do not come spontaneously imposed from the unknown, but depend in their origin on firmly established foundations of thinking and deduction.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a theory often associated with positivist or empiricist epistemology, which posits three stages of knowledge: sensory, scientific, and philosophical. The author first explains the theory with an example from medicine: a patient observes that a herb cures his headache (sensory stage), then after repeated trials infers a general law (scientific stage), and finally philosophical knowledge synthesizes laws from multiple sciences. The author then offers three criticisms, each building on the necessity of rational principles. The first critique emphasizes that experiment alone cannot produce a scientific law without the 'intellectual criterion' (al-mizan al-‘aqli), which corresponds to the rational faculty that makes inferences. The author uses a strong argument: even a million repetitions of an experiment do not yield a universal law unless reason steps in. This mirrors the Islamic epistemological principle that sense perception is insufficient for certain knowledge without ‘aql. The second critique distinguishes philosophical knowledge from scientific knowledge: philosophy deals with generalities not bound by physical laws and is not derived from material experiment. Therefore, scientific knowledge does not automatically lead to philosophical knowledge, contrary to the theory's assumption. The third critique asserts that all three stages depend on primary rational principles (al-mabadi’ al-‘aqliyya al-awwaliyya), which are foundational and not imposed from the unknown. These principles are the basis for thinking and deduction. Thus, the author refutes the idea that knowledge accumulates solely from sensory experience up to philosophy, highlighting the essential role of a priori rational principles.

Key Terms
الميزان العقلي
المباديء العقلية الاولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first criticism of the three-stage theory, why can experiment alone not produce a scientific law?
A) Experiments are too few.
B) Experiment needs the intellectual criterion (al-mizan al-‘aqli).
C) Sensory stage must be completed first.
D) Philosophical knowledge is derived from experiment.

Key Takeaway
The three-stage theory fails to acknowledge that rational principles are necessary for experiment to yield laws and for scientific knowledge to ascend to philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In usul al-fiqh, the derivation of a legal ruling (hukm) from textual evidence requires ijtihad, which involves rational principles such as qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istihsan. Explain how the author's critique of the three-stage theory applies to the claim that mere observation of patterns in revelation (like repeated commands) can produce a general legal principle without rational inference.
?
Task 2: Consider the argument: 'The sun rises every day; therefore it will rise tomorrow.' According to the first criticism, why is this inference not valid without a rational principle? Relate this to the Islamic concept of 'ada (customary practice) and its epistemological status in kalam.
